<li>If this does not work, repeat step 1 and follow with step 2 until the obstruction is dislodged.</li>
<li>If the back slaps and abdominal thrusts do not appear to be working, shout for someone to call 999 for an ambulance, but do not stop administering the treatment if the casualty is conscious.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="choking-in-a-baby-under-1-year">Choking in a baby under 1 year<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#choking-in-a-baby-under-1-year" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The baby may attempt to cough on their own. If the choking is not serious, this will clear the obstruction. The baby may cry which indicates they are now breathing properly.</p>
<p>If the obstruction is not cleared by coughing, follow the steps below:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="9e085996">1 – Back slaps<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9e085996" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Shout for help immediately, but do not leave the baby alone.</li>
<li>Lay the baby over your arm facing downwards with their legs either side of your elbow with their head below their chest.</li>
<li>Administer up to five slaps firmly between the shoulder blades with the palms of your fingers, not your open hand.</li>
<li>Check between each slap if the obstruction has dislodged.</li>
<li>If this does not work go to step 2.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-19f86512.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>2 – Chest thrusts</p>
<ul>
<li>Turn the baby over, so they are laying chest up on your other arm, keep their head below their chest.</li>
<li>Using two fingers on the baby’s chest give up to 5 chest thrusts. This is a similar manoeuvre to chest compressions in CPR, but sharper and administered at a slower rate.</li>
<li>Check between each thrust if the obstruction has dislodged.</li>
<li>If this does not work, repeat step 1 and follow with step 2 until the obstruction is dislodged.</li>
</ul>
<p>Never administer abdominal thrusts on a baby.</p>
<p>If the obstruction has still not dislodged repeat steps 1 and 2.</p>
<p>If the casualty becomes unconscious make sure they are laying on the ground (or on a flat firm surface for a baby) and commence CPR. Make sure there is an ambulance on the way. Continue CPR until help arrives or you become fatigued.</p>
<p>Anaphylactic shock</p>
<p>Anaphylaxis is an extreme allergic reaction which can be fatal. This is triggered by a massive over reaction by the immune system. Severe anaphylactic reaction is a rare occurrence, usually triggered by drugs such as penicillin, insect stings, nuts such as peanuts and shellfish such as prawns, latex, dairy produce, etc.</p>
<p>When the body detects a ‘foreign protein’ the immune cells release histamine. Histamine can have the following effects on the body if released in massive quantities:</p>
<ul>
<li>Dilates blood vessels.</li>
<li>Constricts the bronchioles in lungs.</li>
<li>Makes blood capillary walls weaken and leak, which causes severe swelling and shock.</li>
<li>Weakens the heart’s contractions.</li>
<li>Makes the skin itchy and come out in a rash.</li>
</ul>
<p>Signs and symptoms</p>
<p>An allergic reaction can occur in seconds, so recognising the problem is essential:</p>
<ul>
<li>Casualty’s face, tongue, neck, lips and eyes may suddenly swell up.</li>
<li>Their voice may become hoarse, developing a ‘lump in the throat’ which may lead to loud, noisy breathing which may stop altogether.</li>
<li>Tightness in the chest, difficulty breathing, wheezing (the casualty may have</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 22</p>
<p>the equivalent of an asthma attack, with the addition of a swollen airway).</p>
<ul>
<li>Weak, rapid pulse.</li>
<li>Nausea, stomach cramps, vomiting, diarrhoea.</li>
<li>Itchy, red or blotchy skin.</li>
<li>Anxiety, a feeling of impending doom.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-anaphylactic-shock">Treatment of anaphylactic shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-anaphylactic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Lay the casualty in as comfortable position as possible. If the casualty is having problems breathing, they may want to sit up to ease this.</li>
<li>If the casualty is feeling faint, do not let them sit up. Keep them lying flat and raise their legs.</li>
<li>If the casualty is aware of their condition, they may be carrying an adrenaline shot. This can save the casualty’s life if administered promptly.</li>
<li>The casualty can usually give themselves the adrenaline shot, but if they are unable you may have to help them.</li>
<li>If the casualty becomes unconscious, check their airway and breathing and resuscitate as necessary.</li>
<li>The adrenaline shot (epinephrine) can be given again if there is no improvement, or symptoms return after five minutes.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="asthma">Asthma<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asthma" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Asthma is a fairly common allergic reaction in the lungs, usually caused by pollution, dust, pollen or traffic fumes. The muscles surrounding the bronchioles spasm and constrict which makes it very difficult for the casualty to breathe. Asthma sufferers normally carry around medication in the form of an inhaler which when breathed in dilates the bronchioles helping to relieve the condition.</p>
<p>Asthma attacks can be very traumatic for the casualty, especially children, so be sure to reassure them and keep them as calm as possible. This is best achieved by being calm yourself and let them know you are a first aider. If the casualty is not calmed in a timely manner, their attack may lead to ‘hyperventilation’ after the inhaler has been used and their breathing has eased.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Difficulty in breathing.</li>
<li>Wheezy breathing, originating in the lungs.</li>
<li>Difficulty with speaking (needing breath in the middle of a sentence).</li>
<li>Clammy, pale skin.</li>
<li>Cyanosis, blue or greyish colour to the lips and skin.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Use of muscles in the upper chest and neck help the casualty to breath.</li>
<li>If it is a severe attack, the casualty may become exhausted.</li>
<li>If the attack is prolonged, the casualty may become unconscious and stop breathing.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-an-asthma-attack">Treatment of an asthma attack<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-an-asthma-attack" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sit the casualty upright, with their back to a wall, table or chair for support.</li>
<li>Help the casualty to use their inhaler (usually a blue inhaler for an attack). This can be administered every few minutes, if the attack does not abate.</li>
<li>Keep talking to the casualty, reassuring them and keep them calm. Ask them simple questions; keep their mind off the attack.</li>
<li>Should the attack be severe, prolonged, appears to be getting worse or the casualty is becoming exhausted <strong>dial 999 for an ambulance</strong>.</li>
<li>Frigid, winter air can worsen an attack, so do not take the casualty outside for fresh air.</li>
<li>Keep the casualty sitting upright while they are conscious, even if they become too weak to sit upright on their own. Only ever lay a casualty having an asthma attack down if they become unconscious, then place them in the recovery position and be prepared to carry out resuscitation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="croup">Croup<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#croup" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a condition usually suffered by infants, where the larynx and trachea become infected and swell. These attacks usually occur during the night and can be very alarming but usually pass without any lasting harm being done to the child.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Distressed, difficult breathing</li>
<li>A loud pitched or whistling sound as the casualty breathes.</li>
<li>A short ‘barking’ cough.</li>
<li>Clammy, pale skin.</li>
<li>Cyanosis, blue or greyish colour to the lips and skin.</li>
<li>Use of muscles in the upper chest and neck help the casualty to breathe.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-croup">Treatment of croup<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-croup" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Keep calm as panic will distress the child and worsen the attack.</li>
<li>Sit the child upright and keep reassuring them.</li>
<li>Call a doctor.</li>
<li>If the attack is a severe one, does not ease or the child is running a temperature, <strong>dial 999 for an ambulance</strong>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Never try to put your fingers down the child’s throat as there is a small chance that the condition could be ‘epiglottitis’. If it is then the epiglottis may swell even more and totally block the child’s airway.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hyperventilation">Hyperventilation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hyperventilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hyperventilation means ‘excessive breathing’. When we breathe in we take in a trace amount of carbon dioxide and when we breathe out this rises to about 4% carbon dioxide. Hyperventilation results in low levels of carbon dioxide in the blood which is what causes the symptoms of this condition.</p>
<p>Hyperventilation attacks can be brought on by anxiety, a panic attack or a sudden fright, and can be confused with an asthma attack. Asthma sufferers may hyperventilate after using their inhalers, once their airway has opened. The difference can be told by the large amounts of air being taken in by the hyperventilating casualty compared to the tight, wheezing breath of the asthma casualty.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Unnaturally fast deep breathing.</li>
<li>Dizziness and faintness.</li>
<li>Complaining of a ‘tight’ chest.</li>
<li>Cramping in the hands and feet.</li>
<li>Flush skin, and no signs of cyanosis.</li>
<li>Pins and needles in the arms and hands.</li>
<li>The casualty may feel they can’t breathe.</li>
<li>A prolonged hyperventilation attack may result in the casualty passing out, and may stop breathing for up to 30 seconds.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-hyperventilation">Treatment of hyperventilation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-hyperventilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Reassure the casualty, but be firm and stay calm.</li>
<li>Move them to a quiet, preferably isolated area.</li>
<li>Explain to the casualty that they are hyperventilating and need to calm down.</li>
<li>Try to coach their breathing, slowing it and calming them.</li>
<li>Ask the casualty to take tiny sips of water. This will reduce the amount of breaths they can take.</li>
<li>Ask them to breathe through their nose, as this reduces the loss of carbon dioxide. They may need lots of reassurance to do this.</li>
<li>If the attack continues, or you are in any doubt, seek medical advice.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drowning">Drowning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drowning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is a misconception that drowning victims breathe in a large amount of water.</p>
<p>Page | 25
This document is available on
© studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>In truth, 90% of drowning fatalities are caused by a relatively small amount of water in the lungs which interferes with the oxygen exchange in the alveoli (known as wet drowning). The other 10% are caused by spasms in the muscles near the epiglottis and larynx which blocks the airway (known as dry drowning). The casualty will have swallowed a large amount of water, which may be vomited during resuscitation.</p>
<p>Please remember that drowning can have many factors such as alcohol, hypothermia or a medical condition such as heart attack or epilepsy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="secondary-drowning">Secondary Drowning:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-drowning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When a small amount of water is taken into the lungs it causes irritation and fluid is drawn from the blood into the alveoli. This reaction could happen several hours after a near drowning, the casualty may relapse after appearing to have recovered fully and have difficulty breathing later on. This is why any drowning casualty who has been resuscitated should be taken to hospital as a matter of urgency.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-drowning">Treatment of drowning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-drowning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Firstly, do not endanger yourself as you getting in trouble won't help the casualty. Try not to enter the water yourself unless you have been trained to do so. If possible, try to reach the casualty with a stick, rope or a floating object (lifesavers or similar).</p>
<ul>
<li>Do not put yourself at risk. Try to reach the casualty with a rope, stick or float.</li>
<li>Try to keep the casualty horizontal during the rescue as they may go into shock.</li>
<li>Check their airway and breathing. Perform CPR if necessary.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance. Do this even if they appear to have fully recovered, as secondary drowning may occur.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="collapsed-lung-sucking-chest-wound">Collapsed lung / sucking chest wound<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collapsed-lung-sucking-chest-wound" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The lungs are surrounded by two layers of membrane, known as the 'pleura'. Between the membranes is a 'pleural cavity' which contains a very thin layer of 'serous fluid'. This fluid enables the layers to move against each other as we breathe.</p>
<p>A casualty with a penetrating chest injury will have had the outer layer of the pleura damaged. This causes air to be sucked in from the outside of the chest into the pleural cavity which in turn causes the lung to collapse (pneumothorax).</p>
<p>With any serious chest injury the inner layer of the pleura may become perforated, which will cause air to be drawn from the lung into the pleural</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>cavity causing the lung to collapse. If air is continuously drawn into the pleural cavity, but is unable to escape, pressure will build in the collapsed lung (tension pneumothorax). This pressure may squeeze both the heart and uninjured lung preventing both from functioning properly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Severe difficulty breathing.</li>
<li>Cyanosis of skin and lips (grey or bluish colouration).</li>
<li>Painful breathing.</li>
<li>Clammy, pale skin.</li>
<li>Breathing is fast and shallow.</li>
<li>Chest will not move symmetrically as the injured side may not rise.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="if-there-is-a-sucking-chest-wound">If there is a sucking chest wound<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#if-there-is-a-sucking-chest-wound" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sound of air being drawn into the wound along with bubbling blood.</li>
<li>‘Crackling’ feeling to the skin around the wound due to air entry.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-collapsed-lung-sucking-chest-wound">Treatment of collapsed lung / sucking chest wound<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-collapsed-lung-sucking-chest-wound" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Immediately cover the wound with either your, or the casualty’s hand (if they are conscious) to help prevent air being sucked in.</li>
<li>Dial 999 immediately for an ambulance. Ask someone to do this if you are not alone.</li>
<li>Place a sterile pad over the wound and cover with plastic (cling film, kitchen foil or any other air tight covering will do).</li>
<li>Tape the plastic covering on three sides only, as you want to stop air getting in but not getting out.</li>
<li>If the casualty loses consciousness, open the airway and check their breathing.</li>
<li>Perform CPR if necessary. If they are breathing, place them in the recovery position with the injured lung lowest, to help protect the uninjured lung.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="flail-chest">Flail chest<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#flail-chest" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This refers to a condition where the ribs surrounding the chest have been fractured in several places creating a ‘floating’ section in the chest wall.</p>
<p>As the casualty draws breath the chest moves normally, but the flail section will move inwards and outwards when the rest of the chest is moving outwards and inwards. These are known as paradoxical chest movements.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Severe breathing difficulties.</li>
<li>Painful, shallow breathing.</li>
<li>Same signs and symptoms of a fracture.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
© studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Paradoxical chest movement.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-flail-chest">Treatment of flail chest<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-flail-chest" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 immediately for an ambulance.</li>
<li>Place the casualty in the most comfortable position for them, preferably sat up and inclined towards the injury.</li>
<li>Place large amounts of padding over the flail area.</li>
<li>Put the arm of the injured side in an elevated sling, squeezing the arm gently against the padding to help provide gentle, firm support to the injury.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-circulatory-system">The circulatory system<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-circulatory-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The circulatory system consists of a closed network of tubes (arteries, veins and capillaries) which are all connected to a pump (the heart).</p>
<p>Arteries carry the blood away from the heart. They have strong, muscular, elastic walls which expand as the blood from the heart surges through them. The largest artery is the 'aorta', which connects directly to the heart.</p>
<p>Veins carry the blood towards the heart. Their walls are thinner than artery walls as the blood they carry is under less pressure. They have one way valves to keep the blood flowing towards the heart. The largest veins are the 'vena cava', which connect to the heart.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b1f9cba3.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Capillaries are tiny blood vessels which float between the arteries and veins and carry oxygen, carbon dioxide and nutrients in and out of the body’s cells.</p>
<p>The heart is basically a four chambered pump. The left and right sides are separate. The left side of the heart takes oxygenated blood from the lungs and pumps it around the body, whereas the right side of the heart takes blood from the body and pumps it to the lungs.</p>
<p>The two sides are separated into two chambers known as the ‘atria’ and the ‘ventricles’. The atria are the top chambers which collect blood as it returns from both the lungs and the body, pumping it to the ventricles. The ventricles in turn pump the blood out of the heart to the lungs and around the body.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-blood">The blood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-blood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>60% of blood is made up of a clear yellow fluid called ‘plasma’. Within the plasma are red blood cells, white blood cells, platelets and nutrients.</p>
<p>Red cells contain haemoglobin, which carries oxygen that is used by the body’s cells. Red cells give blood its colour.</p>
<p>White cells are what help us fight infections.</p>
<p>Platelets trigger complicated chemical reactions if a blood vessel is damaged forming a clot.</p>
<p>Nutrients are derived from food by the digestive system. When nutrients are combined with oxygen within the cells of the body they provide energy, keeping the cells alive.</p>
<ul>
<li>The blood carries carbon dioxide (waste gas produced by the cells) in the form of ‘carbonic acid’, which is diluted within the plasma.</li>
<li>The blood also circulates heat (generated mostly by the liver). This heat is carried to the skin by the blood if the body needs cooling down.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-pulse">The pulse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-pulse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Whenever the heart contracts, blood is pumped through the arteries. The elastic walls of the arteries expand as the blood flows through them, which can be felt wherever arteries come close to the skin.</p>
<p>When you check a pulse use the pads of your fingers not your thumb, as thumbs have their own pulse. The first aider should make a note of the following when checking for a pulse:</p>
<p>Page | 29</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Rate – Is the pulse slow or fast? Count how many beats there are in a minute.</p>
<p>Rhythm – Is there a regular pulse? Are there any beats missed?</p>
<p>Strength – Is the pulse strong or weak?</p>
<p>The main areas you will find a pulse are in the neck (carotid pulse), the wrist (radial pulse) and in the upper arm (brachial pulse).</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age</th><th>Normal heart rate at rest</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Adult</td><td>60 - 90 bpm</td></tr><tr><td>Child</td><td>90 - 110 bpm</td></tr><tr><td>Baby</td><td>110 - 140 bpm</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="capillary-refill">Capillary refill<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#capillary-refill" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Circulation to the end of the arms and legs can be momentarily checked by squeezing the tip of a finger or toe. The skin will appear pale when squeezed – if the circulation is working properly the colour will return within two seconds when released. This process can take a little longer if the hands or feet are cold.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="angina">Angina<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#angina" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Angina (angina pectoris) is a condition that is usually caused by the build-up of cholesterol plaque on the lining of a coronary artery. Cholesterol is a fatty chemical that is part of the outer lining of cells in the body. Cholesterol plaque is a hard, thick substance which builds up from the deposits of cholesterol on the artery wall. Over time the build-up of cholesterol plaque causes the arteries to narrow and harden.</p>
<p>When we exercise or get excited the heart requires more oxygen, but the narrowed arteries are not able to increase the blood supply that is being demanded. The result of this is an area of the heart will suffer from a lack of oxygen. The casualty will feel pain in the chest as a result.</p>
<p>Usually angina attacks occur with exertion but subside with rest. However, if the narrowing of the artery reaches a critical level angina may occur during rest (known as ‘unstable angina’). Casualties with angina, especially unstable angina, are at a high risk of suffering from a heart attack in the near future.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heart-attack">Heart attack<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heart-attack" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A heart attack (myocardial infarction) is usually caused when the surface of a cholesterol plaque build-up in a coronary artery cracks and develops a ‘rough surface’. This may lead to a blood clot forming on the plaque which in turn completely blocks the artery resulting in the death of an area of the heart muscle.</p>
<p>However, unlike angina, the death of the heart muscle from a heart attack is permanent and will not be eased with rest.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Please bear in mind that each heart attack is different. They may not show all the signs below, in fact up to a quarter of heart attacks are ‘silent’ and happen without any chest pain.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Angina</th><th>Heart Attack</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Onset</td><td>Sudden, during exertion, stress or extreme weather.</td><td>Sudden and can occur at rest.</td></tr><tr><td>Pain</td><td>‘Vicelike’ pain, can be described as ‘dull’, ‘tightness’ or ‘pressure’ in the chest. May be mistaken for indigestion.</td><td>‘Vicelike’ pain, can be described as ‘dull’, ‘tightness’ or ‘pressure’ in the chest. May be mistaken for indigestion.</td></tr><tr><td>Location of pain</td><td>Central chest area, can radiate to either arm (usually the left), the neck, jaw, back or shoulders.</td><td>Central chest area, can radiate to either arm (usually the left), the neck, jaw, back or shoulders.</td></tr><tr><td>Duration</td><td>Normally lasts 3 to 8 minutes rarely longer.</td><td>Normally lasts more than 30 minutes.</td></tr><tr><td>Skin</td><td>Pale, could be sweaty.</td><td>Pale, ashen, may sweat a lot.</td></tr><tr><td>Pulse</td><td>Varies, depending on which area has the lack of oxygen. Often the pulse is irregular or misses beats.</td><td>Varies, depending on which area has the lack of oxygen. Often the pulse is irregular or misses beats.</td></tr><tr><td>Other signs &amp; symptoms</td><td>Shortness of breath, anxiety and weakness.</td><td>Shortness of breath, dizziness, nausea, vomiting. A sense of ‘impending doom’.</td></tr><tr><td>Factors giving relief</td><td>Rest, reduce stress, taking ‘G.T.N.’ medication.</td><td>Giving ‘G.T.N.’ medication may give partial or no relief.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-angina-heart-attack">Treatment of angina / heart attack<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-angina-heart-attack" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sit the casualty down and make them comfortable. Try to stop them from walking around or doing anything strenuous. Sit them in the Fowler position (or the ‘W’ position). Have them lean against a wall, or your knees if no other option, and raise their knees.</li>
<li>Ask the casualty if they have any medication with them. If they do, allow them to take their own glyceryl tri-nitrate (G.T.N.) medication if they have it. DO NOT GIVE IT TO THEM DIRECTLY BUT HELP THEM SELF MEDICATE.</li>
<li>Reassure the casualty. Remove any causes of stress or anxiety if possible.</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 31
This document is available on
s  t  u  d  o  c  u
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>If you have any reason to suspect a heart attack – check if the casualty is allergic to aspirin, older than 16 or if they are taking any ‘anti-coagulant’ drugs such as warfarin. If all is clear, allow them to chew an aspirin tablet slowly, as this may be beneficial. If, however, you are unsure of any of the above, wait for the ambulance to arrive.</li>
<li>Monitor the casualty. If it is a heart attack and the casualty becomes unconscious it is more than likely the heart has stopped and you will need to perform CPR, so be prepared.</li>
</ul>
<p>Aspirin helps stop clotting in the blood. Having a casualty chew an aspirin tablet allows the drug to be absorbed into the blood stream through the skin of the mouth, helping it work faster. The ideal dose of aspirin is 300mg, but any strength will do in these cases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dial-999-for-an-ambulance-if">Dial 999 for an ambulance if:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dial-999-for-an-ambulance-if" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>You have any reason to suspect it is a heart attack.</li>
<li>The casualty has no history of angina.</li>
<li>The symptoms suffered are different or worse than the casualty’s usual angina attacks.</li>
<li>The pain from an angina attack is not relieved by the casualty’s medication and rest after 15 minutes.</li>
<li>You have any doubts at all. It is always better to be safe than sorry in these situations.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="left-ventricular-failure">Left ventricular failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#left-ventricular-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Left ventricular failure (LVF) is where the left ventricular of the heart loses power and cannot empty itself. The right side of the heart is still working and pumping blood into the lungs. This causes a ‘back pressure’ of blood in the pulmonary veins and arteries in the lungs. Fluid from the back pressure of blood seeps into the alveoli which results in severe breathing difficulties.</p>
<p>The condition may be brought on by a heart attack, chronic heart failure or high blood pressure. Casualties with chronic heart failure more often than not suffer attacks at night.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Severe breathing difficulties.</li>
<li>Crackly, wheezy breathing due to fluid on the lungs.</li>
<li>Pale, sweaty skin.</li>
<li>Cyanosis of the skin/lips (grey or bluish discolouration).</li>
<li>Coughing up frothy, bloody sputum.</li>
<li>Possibly the same signs and symptoms of heart attack.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Casualty needs to sit up to be able to breathe.</li>
<li>Confusion, dizziness and anxiety.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-lvf">Treatment of LVF<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-lvf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sit the casualty upright, with their feet dangling.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance as soon as you can.</li>
<li>If the casualty has it, allow them to take their own G.T.N. medication.</li>
<li>Be prepared to perform CPR as this condition can deteriorate rapidly.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shock">Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The usual association with the word shock is a nasty surprise, an earthquake or electrical shock.</p>
<p>The medical definition of shock is ‘inadequate tissue perfusion, caused by a fall in blood pressure and blood volume’. This means there is an inadequate supply of oxygenated blood to the tissues of the body.</p>
<p>Understanding what shock is can help understand why casualties who are in shock need immediate treatment, or the condition can result in death.</p>
<p>The most common causes of life threatening shock are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hypovolaemic Shock.</li>
<li>Cardiogenic Shock.</li>
<li>Anaphylactic Shock.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypovolaemic-shock">Hypovolaemic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypovolaemic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hypo = low  vol = volume  aemic = blood</p>
<p>Hypovolaemic shock is caused by loss of bodily fluids, the result of which is low blood volume. Hypovolaemic shock is usually caused by:</p>
<ul>
<li>External bleeding.</li>
<li>Internal bleeding.</li>
<li>Burns.</li>
<li>Vomiting and diarrhoea.</li>
<li>Excessive sweating.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Usually the first response is a release of adrenaline which will cause:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pulse rate to rise.</li>
<li>Pale, clammy skin. For dark skinned casualties check the colour of the skin inside the lips.</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 33
This document is available on
s  studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>As the condition deteriorates:</p>
<ul>
<li>Shallow, fast breathing.</li>
<li>Nausea or vomiting.</li>
<li>Weak, rapid pulse.</li>
<li>Dizziness or weakness.</li>
<li>Cyanosis (grey/blue tinge to lips and skin).</li>
<li>Sweating.</li>
</ul>
<p>As the brain receives less oxygen:</p>
<ul>
<li>Deep, sighing breathing (air hunger).</li>
<li>Unconsciousness.</li>
<li>Anxiety, confusion, possible aggression.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-hypovolaemic-shock">Treatment of Hypovolaemic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-hypovolaemic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Try to treat the cause of the shock (e.g. external bleeding).</li>
<li>Lay the casualty on a flat surface (preferably the floor) and raise their legs so they are above the chest (heart). This will cause the blood to return to the vital organs as 40% of the body’s blood is in the legs. Take care if you suspect a fracture.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Keep the casualty warm – place a blanket or coat under the casualty if they are on the floor or other cold surface. However, be careful not to overheat them as this dilates the blood vessels which will cause their blood pressure to drop further.</li>
<li>Do not allow the casualty to drink, eat or smoke. Nil by mouth is best!</li>
<li>Loosen any tight clothing such as ties and belts.</li>
<li>Monitor the casualty’s breathing, pulse and response levels.</li>
<li>Be prepared to perform CPR.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cardiogenic-shock">Cardiogenic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cardiogenic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This form of shock occurs when there is a fall in blood pressure caused by the heart not pumping properly. This is the most common type of shock.</p>
<p>Typical causes of cardiogenic shock are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Heart attack.</li>
<li>Tension pneumothorax.</li>
<li>Cardiac failure.</li>
<li>Cardiac arrest.</li>
<li>Heart valve disease.</li>
</ul>
<p>Signs, symptoms and treatment of cardiogenic shock
See section on heart conditions (page 30).</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Page | 35</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anaphylactic-shock">Anaphylactic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anaphylactic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Anaphylaxis is an extremely dangerous allergic reaction which is brought on by a massive over-reaction of the body’s immune system (see page 22).</p>
<p>An anaphylactic reaction may result in shock due to a large quantity of histamine. This can result in:</p>
<ul>
<li>Blood vessels dilating which causes blood pressure to fall.</li>
<li>Blood capillary walls may become ‘leaky’ causing blood volume to fall.</li>
<li>Weakening of the heart’s contractions which causes blood pressure to fall.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-symptoms-and-treatment">Signs, symptoms and treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-symptoms-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>See section on anaphylaxis (see page 22)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fainting">Fainting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fainting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This reaction is caused by poor nervous control of the blood vessels and the heart.</p>
<p>When a casualty faints the blood vessels in the lower body usually dilate which slows the heart. This results in falling blood pressure and the casualty has a temporary reduction in blood supply to the brain.</p>
<p>Typical causes of fainting are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Fright or pain.</li>
<li>Extended periods of inactivity (such as standing or sitting).</li>
<li>Lack of food.</li>
<li>Emotional stress.</li>
<li>Heat exhaustion.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Temporary loss of consciousness resulting in falling to the floor.</li>
<li>Before fainting the casualty may have had nausea, blurred vision, stomach ache or dizziness.</li>
<li>Slow pulse.</li>
<li>Clammy, pale skin.</li>
<li>Quick to recover.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-fainting">Treatment of fainting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-fainting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Lay the casualty on a flat surface, preferably the floor and raise their legs which will return the blood to the vital organs and raise blood pressure.</li>
<li>Check the casualty’s airway and breathing.</li>
<li>Try to remove the cause of stress such as people crowding the casualty and allow plenty of fresh air.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reassure the casualty as they come to. Try to stop them from sitting up suddenly.</li>
<li>If the casualty feels faint again, repeat the treatment and check for an underlying cause.</li>
<li>If the casualty does not recover in a short amount of time and remain unconscious, or you are unsure: check the airway and breathing again, place them in the recovery position and dial 999 for an ambulance.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="wounds-and-bleeding">Wounds and bleeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wounds-and-bleeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A wound is an abnormal break in the continuity of the tissues of the body. Any wound will, to a greater or lesser extent, result in either internal or external bleeding. Severe blood loss could result in shock, so it is important to treat wounds promptly. There are several types of wound – identification and treatment are detailed here.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="types-and-basic-treatment-of-wounds">Types and basic treatment of wounds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-and-basic-treatment-of-wounds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Contusion</strong> is a bruise. Contusions are caused by ruptured capillaries bleeding under the skin. Typically these are caused by a blow or by bleeds caused by an underlying problem such as a fracture.</p>
<ul>
<li>Put an ice pack on the affected area, or place the area under cold running water as soon as possible.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Abrasion</strong> is a graze. This is the result of the top layer of skin being scraped off, usually as the result of a sliding fall or a friction burn. Abrasions can often contain particles of dirt which could lead to infection.</p>
<ul>
<li>Any dirt that is not embedded in the graze should be removed with clean water and sterile swabs.</li>
<li>Always clean from the centre of the wound outwards to reduce the risk of introducing more dirt into the wound.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Laceration</strong> is a rip or tear in the skin. These are more likely to have particles of dirt than a cut but tend to bleed less.</p>
<ul>
<li>Treat as a bleed (see page 36) and prevent infection.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Incision</strong> is a clean cut. These wounds are usually caused by a sharp object, such as broken glass or a knife. If the wound is deep it could provide complications such as severed tendons or blood vessels. These wounds tend to bleed freely and may even ‘gape open’.</p>
<ul>
<li>Treat as a bleed (see page 36) and prevent infection.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Puncture</strong> is a stab wound. These wounds can be caused by such implements as</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>a nail or actually being stabbed. The wound is likely to be deep but may appear to be small in diameter. Damage may be deep, hitting underlying organs such as the lungs or heart, and may cause severe internal bleeding.</p>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance if you suspect the wound has penetrated deep enough to damage any organs or cause internal bleeding.</li>
<li>If the object is embedded in the puncture do not remove it as it may be stemming the bleed, and removal may cause further damage.</li>
</ul>
<p>Gun shot is caused by a bullet or other missile travelling at a high enough speed to drive into and possibly exit the body. There may be a small entry wound and a larger, 'crater' exit wound. Severe damage to internal organs should be assumed, and will be accompanied by severe bleeding.</p>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 for both an ambulance and the police.</li>
<li>Clear the casualty's airway and check for breathing first. Be prepared to commence CPR.</li>
<li>Pack the wound with dressings if possible to prevent further bleeding.</li>
</ul>
<p>Amputation is the complete or partial removal of a limb.</p>
<ul>
<li>See the section on amputation (see page 43).</li>
</ul>
<p>De-gloved is the severing of the skin from the body, which results in a 'creasing' or a flap of skin coming away and leaving a bare area of tissue. These wounds are usually caused by the force of an object sliding along the length of the skin, in effect skinning it.</p>
<ul>
<li>If possible, put the skin back in place.</li>
<li>Arrange transport to hospital urgently.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="blood-loss">Blood loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-much-blood-does-a-body-have">How much blood does a body have?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-much-blood-does-a-body-have" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The amount of blood in a body varies depending on the size of the person. However, a rough guide is that we have approximately a pint of blood for every stone in weight (0.5 litres per 7kg) so the average adult will have between 8 and 12 pints (4.5 to 6.5 litres) of blood depending on their size. However, this rule does not work for someone who is overweight.</p>
<p>Bear in mind that children have less blood than adults and cannot afford to lose anywhere near the same amount. A baby has only approximately 1 pint of blood and can only afford to lose 1/3 of a pint before their blood pressure falls.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-bleeding">Types of bleeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-bleeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Arterial bleeds tend to spurt from wounds in time with the heart-beat, as blood in the arteries is under direct pressure from the heart. A wound to a major artery</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>may result in the blood ‘spurting’ several meters instantly with the blood volume rapidly reducing over time. Blood in the arteries is highly oxygenated and will be bright red, however this may be difficult to assess so do not rely on it as a form of identification. More importantly is how the wound is bleeding.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5e783916.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p><em>Venous</em> wounds are damage to veins which carry blood that is not under direct pressure from the heart, but may carry the same volume of blood as the arteries. Wounds to a major vein could ‘ooze’ profusely.</p>
<p><em>Capillary</em> bleeds occur in all wounds. These bleeds may appear fast at first, blood loss form capillary bleeds tend to be slight and is easily controlled. Capillary bleeds tend to be described as a ‘trickle’.</p>
<p><em>Dealing with wounds hygienically</em></p>
<ul>
<li>Be sure to protect yourself by covering any of your own cuts or abrasions with a waterproof dressing, especially if they are on your hands or arms.</li>
<li>If they are available, wear disposable gloves and apron when administering first aid to wounds.</li>
<li>Use specific cleaning products for cleaning up bodily fluids. Always follow the instructions and use disposable towels.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Always dispose of soiled dressings or disposable towels used to clean up bodily fluid in a yellow ‘clinical waste’ container. These need to be taken away and incinerated (send the container to the hospital with the casualty if you have no clinical waste facilities where you are).</li>
<li>Always wash your hands thoroughly before and after dealing with a casualty. This helps reduce the risk of infection.</li>
<li>If you are dealing with body fluids on a regular basis, ask your doctor about vaccination against hepatitis ‘B’.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-effects-of-blood-loss">The effects of blood loss<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-effects-of-blood-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Please see the table below for the effects, signs and symptoms of blood loss. The table gives the volume of blood loss as a percentage as we all have different quantities of blood, depending on the size of the person.</p>
<p>Please note that a casualty who has lost 30% of their blood is in a critical condition, and will deteriorate rapidly from this point onwards. Blood vessels cannot constrict anymore and the heart cannot beat any faster so their blood pressure will fall, resulting in unconsciousness and death.</p>
<p>Also, please be aware that any casualty who has lost over 10% of their blood should be treated for shock (see page 33). See also hypovolaemic shock (see page 33/4) and hypoxia (see page 16).</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>10% Blood loss</th><th>20% Blood loss</th><th>30% Blood loss</th><th>40% + Blood loss</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Consciousness</td><td>Normal</td><td>Could feel dizzy while standing</td><td>Lowered levels of consciousness. Restless &amp; anxious</td><td>Unresponsive</td></tr><tr><td>Skin</td><td>Normal</td><td>Pale</td><td>Cyanosis (blue/grey tinge of skin &amp; lips), cold and clammy</td><td>Severe cyanosis, cold and clammy</td></tr><tr><td>Pulse</td><td>Normal (this is the usual amount taken when donating)</td><td>Slightly raised</td><td>Rapid (over 100 bpm), hard to detect</td><td>Undetectable</td></tr><tr><td>Breathing</td><td>Normal</td><td>Slightly raised</td><td>Rapid</td><td>Deep sighing breaths (air hunger)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-external-bleeding">Treatment of external bleeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-external-bleeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The aim of treating external bleeding is firstly to stop the bleed, then prevent the casualty from going into shock and finally to prevent infection.</p>
<p>The acronym SEEP should help you to remember the following steps:</p>
<p>Sit or lay Sit or lay the casualty down, ensuring they are in a position that is appropriate for the location of the wound.</p>
<p>Page | 39
This document is available on
s  studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Examine Examine the wound for foreign objects and make a note of how the wound is bleeding. Make sure to tell the medical staff how the wound was bleeding once it is covered with a bandage.</p>
<p>Elevate Elevate the wound so that it is above the heart, which will use gravity to restrict the flow of blood to the injury.</p>
<p>Pressure Apply direct or indirect pressure to help slow the bleeding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="direct-pressure">Direct pressure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#direct-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Direct pressure to the wound is the best way of stemming a bleed. You can use your hands to do this, but you should take precautions to minimise the risk of coming in contact with the casualty's blood, preferably by wearing disposable gloves. Keep pressure on the wound continuously for at least ten minutes. Using a firm bandage usually stops the bleeding with most minor wounds (make sure the bandage is not so tight as to cut off the circulation to the limb altogether). If there is a foreign object embedded in the wound do not remove it but you may be able to apply pressure at either side of the object.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="indirect-pressure">Indirect pressure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#indirect-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If it is not possible or effective to apply direct pressure to a wound, you can use indirect pressure as a last resort. This is achieved by applying pressure to the artery which is supplying blood to the limb, pressing it against the bone beneath, reducing the blood flow. This should be done for a maximum of ten minutes.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-63d28bb7.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>There are two indirect pressure points:</p>
<p><strong>Brachial</strong> This artery runs along the inside of the upper arm. To help with this, ask the casualty to make a fist with the opposite hand and place it under the arm pit of the injured arm and ask them to squeeze down on the fist.</p>
<p><strong>Femoral</strong> This artery is located where the thigh bone (femur) crosses the 'bikini' line. If applying indirect pressure here, be sure to explain briefly to the casualty what you are doing and why. One way of applying indirect pressure</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>here is to use the heel of your foot.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dressings">Dressings:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dressings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dressings should be sterile and just large enough to cover the wound. They should be made out of a material that will not stick to the clotting blood and be absorbent (a ‘non-adherent’ dressing).</p>
<p>Usually, a firmly applied dressing is enough to stem bleeding from the majority of minor wounds, but any dressing should not restrict the flow of blood to the rest of the limb (you can check the circulation with a ‘capillary refill’ test, see page 30).</p>
<p>If the bleeding is severe, it may be necessary to apply direct pressure by hand and elevate the wound. If the dressing becomes soaked with blood, put a larger dressing on the top. If the bleeding continues and soaks the second dressing, remove both dressings and start again. This is to ensure any bacteria is removed from the wound with the first bandage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="embedded-objects">Embedded objects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#embedded-objects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
