<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objects-embedded-in-a-wound">Objects embedded in a wound:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objects-embedded-in-a-wound" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>If there is an object embedded in the wound (other than a small splinter) you should not attempt to remove it as it may be stemming a severe bleed, or further damage may result.</p>
<p>Use sterile dressings and bandages to build up around the object, which will supply the pressure needed to stem the bleed and help support the object. Take the casualty to hospital to have the object removed safely.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="splinters">Splinters:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#splinters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>If there is a splinter deeply embedded, difficult to remove or in a joint, do not try to remove it but follow the advice for embedded objects as above. If the splinter can be removed, follow the below advice:</p>
<ul>
<li>Carefully use warm soapy water to clean the area.</li>
<li>Use a clean pair of tweezers to grip the splinter as close to the visible base as possible. Gently pull the splinter out at the same angle it entered at.</li>
<li>Gently squeeze around the wound to encourage a little bleeding then wash the wound and cover with a dry dressing.</li>
<li>Ask the casualty if they have had a tetanus shot in the last ten years, if not suggest they get one as soon as possible.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objects-embedded-in-the-ears-nose-or-other-orifice">Objects embedded in the ears, nose or other orifice:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objects-embedded-in-the-ears-nose-or-other-orifice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>If there is a foreign body stuck in a casualty’s ear, nose or other orifice, do not</p>
<p>Page | 41</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>attempt to remove it. Take them directly to hospital where it will be safely removed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nose-bleeds">Nose bleeds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nose-bleeds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nose bleeds are usually the result of weakened or dried out blood tissues in the nose. A nose bleed can be triggered by a bang to the nose, picking or blowing it. However, it can be the symptom of a more serious problem such as high blood pressure or a fractured skull.</p>
<ul>
<li>Have the casualty sit with their head tipped forward to allow the blood to drain.</li>
<li>Gently pinch the soft part of the nose, and maintain constant pressure for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Tell the casualty to breathe through their mouth.</li>
<li>Give the casualty some tissues or a cloth to clean up the blood while you maintain pressure on the soft part of the nose.</li>
<li>Once the bleeding has stopped, recommend to the casualty that they try to continue breathing through their mouth and not blow their nose for the next couple of hours as this may trigger another nose bleed.</li>
<li>However, if the bleeding continues for more than 30 minutes, or they take an 'anti-coagulant' drug (e.g. warfarin), get them to hospital ensuring they stay in an upright position.</li>
<li>If the casualty has a history of frequent nose bleeds, recommend they visit their doctor to determine why.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="eye-injury">Eye injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eye-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If there are small particles of dust or dirt in the eye, this can be easily washed out using cold tap water. Make sure the water runs away from the good eye.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-0a726a32.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>For more serious eye injuries:</p>
<ul>
<li>Try to keep the casualty still and calm. Gently place a soft, sterile dressing over the injured eye. You can tape it in place if necessary.</li>
<li>Ask the casualty to close both their eyes, as any movement of the good eye will cause the injured eye to move too. If necessary, you can place a bandage over the good eye to prevent it moving. Please be sure to give the casualty lots of reassurance at this point!</li>
</ul>
<p>If you can, take the casualty to A&amp;E. Dial 999 for an ambulance if you cannot take the casualty to A&amp;E yourself.</p>
<p>For chemical eye injuries:
Make sure you are wearing protective gloves if possible. Wash the injured eye with lots of clean tap water, making sure the water runs away from the good eye. Gently, but firmly try to open the casualty’s eye fully to wash it as much as possible. Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="amputation">Amputation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amputation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Amputation is defined as the full or partial severing of a limb, and is extremely traumatic for the casualty. The priority here is to stop the bleeding, then preserve the amputated limb and reassure the casualty.</p>
<ul>
<li>Immediately treat the casualty for bleeding (see page 36) and shock (see page 33).</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Cover the wound with a low adherent, non-fluffy dressing.</li>
<li>If possible, wrap the amputated part in a plastic bag and place on or in a bag of ice to preserve it as much as possible. Do not allow the amputated part to be in contact with the ice or get wet in any way.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="crush-injuries">Crush injuries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#crush-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This type of injury usually occurs on building sites or at road traffic accidents. If the flow of blood to a limb (such as an arm or leg) is restricted by a crushing weight, there is the serious danger of a build-up of toxins in the muscle tissue below the crushing weight.</p>
<p>If the flow of blood is restricted to the limb for more than 15 minutes, the toxins will build to such a level that when the weight is removed, and the toxins released into the body, they may cause kidney failure. This is known as ‘crush syndrome’ and may well result in the death of the casualty.</p>
<p>In these cases expert medical care is essential when releasing the casualty if the</p>
<p>Page | 43
This document is available on
5 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>blood flow has been restricted for 15 minutes or more.</p>
<p>Treatment for crushing lasting less than 15 minutes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Remove the crushing weight, or release the casualty as soon as possible.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Treat any bleeds and cover any open wounds.</li>
<li>Treat the casualty for shock (see page 33), but be sure not to move any injuries.</li>
<li>Maintain their airway and monitor their breathing until help arrives.</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment for crushing lasting more than 15 minutes:</p>
<ul>
<li>DO NOT RELEASE THE CASUALTY.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Give as clear and concise information about the accident as you can.</li>
<li>Maintain their airway and monitor their breathing until help arrives.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="internal-bleeding">Internal bleeding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#internal-bleeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a very serious condition, but it can be very hard to recognise in the early stages. Internal bleeding can be attributed to lung or abdominal injuries, but can also happen spontaneously to someone who appears well, such as a bleeding stomach ulcer or a weak artery.</p>
<p>Even though the blood is not lost from the body, it is lost internally out of veins or arteries and can quickly cause the casualty to go into shock.</p>
<p>Internal bleeding can result in serious, life threatening complications such as a brain haemorrhage or bleeding into the lungs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should be aware and looking for internal bleeding if there are signs of the casualty going into shock, but there is no obvious cause such as external bleeding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="there-may-be">There may be:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#there-may-be" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Shock (see page 33).</li>
<li>Pain or a recent history of pain at the site of the bleed.</li>
<li>Bruising and/or swelling.</li>
<li>Other symptoms at the site of the bleed (such as difficulty with breathing if there is bleeding in the lungs).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-internal-bleeding">Treatment of internal bleeding:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-internal-bleeding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Treat the casualty for shock (see page 33).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="poisons">Poisons<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#poisons" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Poisons can be defined as a liquid, solid or gaseous substance that causes damage to the body when it enters in sufficient quantity.</p>
<p>There are 4 ways a poison can enter the body:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Ingested</strong> The substance is swallowed, either by accident or on purpose.</li>
<li><strong>Inhaled</strong> The substance is breathed in, entering the blood stream very quickly through the alveoli.</li>
<li><strong>Absorbed</strong> The substance comes in contact with skin (see chemical burns, page 49).</li>
<li><strong>Injected</strong> The substance is introduced through the skin directly into tissue or a blood vessel.</li>
</ul>
<p>A poison can be one of two things:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Corrosive</strong> These include acids, bleach, petrol, ammonia, dishwasher powder, turpentine, etc.</li>
<li><strong>Non-Corrosive</strong> These include tablets, plants, drugs, perfume, alcohol, etc.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are a wide variety of signs and symptoms for poisoning, depending on the substance. Some clues you can look for are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Bottles or containers.</li>
<li>Tablets or drugs.</li>
<li>Syringes or drug taking paraphernalia.</li>
<li>Smell on the casualty’s breath.</li>
</ul>
<p>Some other signs that can accompany poisoning may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Nausea, retching or vomiting.</li>
<li>Abdominal pain.</li>
<li>Burns (or a burning sensation) around the area of entry.</li>
<li>Problems breathing.</li>
<li>Hallucinations or confusion.</li>
<li>Headache.</li>
<li>Unconscious. Sometimes the casualty may start fitting.</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 45</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Cyanosis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-for-poisoning">Treatment for poisoning:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-for-poisoning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For a corrosive substance firstly make sure that your safety is secure – is it safe to help the casualty?</p>
<p>Dilute or wash away the substance if possible:</p>
<ul>
<li>Substance on the skin – see chemical burns (see page 49).</li>
<li>Ingested substances – try to get the casualty to rinse their mouth, then give frequent sips of milk or cold water.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Give clear and concise information about the poison if possible. Follow any advice given by the ambulance operator.</li>
<li>If the casualty becomes unconscious, immediately open the airway and check for breathing. If they are not breathing commence CPR using a protective face shield. If the casualty is breathing but unconscious, place them in the recovery position and dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="never-try-to-get-the-casualty-to-vomit-as-this-may-damage-the-airway">Never try to get the casualty to vomit as this may damage the airway.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#never-try-to-get-the-casualty-to-vomit-as-this-may-damage-the-airway" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For non-corrosive substances:</p>
<ul>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Give clear and concise information about the poison if possible. Follow any advice given by the ambulance operator.</li>
<li>If the casualty becomes unconscious, immediately open the airway and check for breathing. If they are not breathing commence CPR using a protective face shield. If the casualty is breathing but unconscious, place them in the recovery position and dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p>It will be of help to the paramedics if you:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pass on the container the substance was in, or pass on any information you may have regarding what the casualty has taken.</li>
<li>Let them know how much was taken, if you are able to ascertain this information.</li>
<li>Let them know when the substance was taken, if you can ascertain this information.</li>
<li>Keep any sample of vomit from the casualty for hospital analysis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="burns-and-scalds">Burns and scalds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#burns-and-scalds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="estimating-the-severity-of-a-burn">Estimating the severity of a burn:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#estimating-the-severity-of-a-burn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There are five factors that affect the seriousness of a burn:</p>
<p>Page | 46</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Burns and scalds are both types of skin damage caused by heat:
Burns: Caused by dry heat, such as from a fire or iron
Scalds: Caused by something wet, such as hot water or steam</p>
<p>Size The larger the area of the burn the more severe it is likely to be. The size is usually given as a percentage of the body's surface area. An easy way to work out the percentage is to compare the size of the burn to the casualty's hand. The casualty's open hand (including the fingers) is the equivalent to 1% of their body area.</p>
<p>Cause The cause of the burn will influence the severity of the burn, for example electrical burns could leave the casualty with internal burns and some chemicals (such as hydrofluoric acid) may cause poisoning to the burns as an added complication.</p>
<p>Age The casualty's age will affect the severity of the burn and how long their recovery rate will be. Babies and young children will burn at a lower temperature than adults will. The elderly will take longer to heal from a burn and they may be more susceptible to infection.</p>
<p>Location The location of the burn may affect the severity, in particular the inhaling of hot gasses will burn the airway resulting in instant death. Burns to the eye may well result in permanent blindness.</p>
<p>Depth The deeper the burn, the more severe it is going to be.</p>
<p>Depth of burns:</p>
<p>Skin consists of three layers, these are the epidermis on the outside, the dermis underneath which lies on a layer of subcutaneous fat.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-19610c74.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>The depth of a burn can be defined as:</p>
<p><strong>Superficial</strong>
This is a burn only to the outer epidermis layer. This is most commonly caused by scalds. The burn looks sore, red and swollen.</p>
<p><strong>Intermediate</strong>
These burns affect both the epidermis and the dermis. These burns look raw and blisters will form.</p>
<p><strong>Full thickness</strong>
With this type of burn, both the epidermis and dermis are burned away completely, leaving the exposed subcutaneous fat or beyond. These burns may appear pale, charred or waxy. The casualty’s nerve endings will have been burned away so pain may well be absent which can mislead both you and the casualty.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes-of-burns-and-treatment">Causes of burns and treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-burns-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Burns can be separated into five different areas, the treatment for each burn will differ slightly depending on the cause.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="electrical-burns">Electrical burns:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrical-burns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These burns are caused by heat from an electrical charge flowing through the bodily tissue. You may be able to determine where the current entered the body, and the point of exit, but there will certainly be deep internal burns which are not visible to the eye between the entry and exit burns. The extent of the internal damage can usually be guessed at by the severity of the entry and exit burns.</p>
<p>An electric shock may well cause cardiac arrest, so be prepared to perform CPR. Remember in this case that the casualty’s airway and breathing are the priority.</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensure that it is safe to approach and help the casualty – never put yourself in harm’s way. Make sure the contact between the casualty and the electrical current is broken.</li>
<li>Make sure to maintain the casualty’s airway and breathing.</li>
<li>Try to irrigate the area of the burn, including the area between the entry and exit burns for at least ten minutes.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Continue treating the casualty as you would for a ‘dry heat’ burn.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Dry heat burns:
These burns are caused by any dry heat source or friction.</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensure it is safe to approach and help the casualty – never put yourself in harm’s way.</li>
<li>Make sure to maintain the casualty’s airway and breathing.</li>
<li>Try to take the heat out of the burn by using cold (preferably running) water for about 10 to 15 minutes. If water is not readily available, any other cold, non-harmful liquid such as milk may be used, as some cooling to the burn is better than none. Do this initially, and move to an area where there is running water available if possible. Be careful not to cool large areas too much as this may cause hypothermia.</li>
<li>If possible, remove any rings, watches, bangles, etc., during cooling as the burned area will swell. If there is any clothing that is not stuck to the burn it may be removed very carefully.</li>
<li>Use a sterile dressing on the burn, making sure it is one that will not stick. Cling film is one of the best ways of dressing a burn, as it doesn’t stick and will stop any infection entering the wound. Remove the first two turns of the roll and apply it lengthwise to the burn. Do not wrap it tightly around the burn as the area may swell and cause further discomfort to the casualty. Secure the cling film with a bandage.</li>
<li>Alternatively, you can use a new, clean plastic bag, low adherent dressing or specialised burn dressings if they are available. However, do not rely on the specialised burn dressing to cool the burn, use cold water to do this.</li>
<li>Dial 999 if the burn appears to be severe or the casualty has breathed in smoke or fumes.</li>
</ul>
<p>Wet heat burns (scalds):
Scalds most commonly occur due to contact with boiling water, but can also be from hot fat or other liquids that reach higher temperature than water.</p>
<ul>
<li>Treat as you would a dry heat burn.</li>
</ul>
<p>Chemical burns:
These are caused by chemicals coming into contact with the skin which either corrode, create heat, or both.</p>
<p>If chemicals are used in your place of work, it is very important for you to learn the correct first aid procedures. Remember, different chemicals require different first aid treatment.</p>
<ul>
<li>Make sure the area is safe, try to contain the chemical and ensure that you will not come into contact with it.</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 49
This document is available on studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>If the chemical is a dry powder, it can be brushed off the casualty's skin before treatment. Make sure that you protect yourself.</li>
<li>Use lots of cold running water to wash the chemicals off the skin. This should be done for a longer period of time than for a thermal burn, at least 20 minutes. Be careful not to wash the chemical onto unaffected areas of the body. Try not to let any contaminated pools of water collect under the casualty.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Take note of what the chemical is and give this information to the ambulance operator if you can.</li>
<li>Carefully remove any contaminated clothing from the casualty while you are washing the burn.</li>
<li>If the chemical is in the casualty's eye, wash it as recommended on page 43, making sure the water runs away from the casualty's uninjured eye.</li>
<li>Some chemicals cannot be safely diluted with water, in this case health &amp; safety regulations insist that an 'antidote' be available in case of an emergency. You and any other first aider should be trained in the use of the antidote.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="radiation-sun-burns">Radiation (sun) burns:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radiation-sun-burns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are most commonly seen as sunburn.</p>
<ul>
<li>Persuade the casualty to get out of the sun, preferably indoors.</li>
<li>Give the casualty frequent sips of water to stave off heat exhaustion (see page 63).</li>
<li>Cool the burns with cold water. If, however, the burns are extensive, cool them under a cool shower or get the casualty to sit in a bath of cool water for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>If the burns are extensive and blistering, or you are unsure, seek medical advice.</li>
<li>If the burns are mild, then you can apply after sun cream or calamine lotion to help soothe the area.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="seek-medical-advice-for-burns-if">Seek medical advice for burns if:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#seek-medical-advice-for-burns-if" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The burn is larger than 1 square inch.</li>
<li>The casualty is a baby or child.</li>
<li>The burn is all the way around a limb.</li>
<li>Any part of the burn appears to be full thickness.</li>
<li>The burn is to the hands, feet, genitals or face.</li>
<li>You are not sure.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="never-do-any-of-the-following-when-burns-are-concerned">Never do any of the following when burns are concerned:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#never-do-any-of-the-following-when-burns-are-concerned" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Burst a blister or blisters (the blisters are there to protect against infection).</li>
<li>Touch the burn.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Apply lotions, ointments or fats to a burn as they may introduce infection and will need to be removed once the casualty is in hospital.</li>
<li>Apply adhesive tape or dressings as the burn may be larger than it first appears.</li>
<li>Remove clothing that is stuck to the wound, as this will invariably cause more damage.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-skeletal-system">The skeletal system<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-skeletal-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are 206 bones in the human skeleton, the functions of which are:</p>
<ul>
<li>To provide support to the body’s soft tissue. This gives the body its shape.</li>
<li>To provide protection for vital organs such as the brain, lungs and spinal cord.</li>
<li>To allow movement by incorporating different types of joints and attachment for muscles.</li>
<li>To produce red blood cells, some white blood cells and platelets within the marrow of bones such as the femur.</li>
<li>To provide a store of minerals and energy, such as calcium and fats.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-e100cd9e.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p>Page | 51</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5 studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="causes-of-injury">Causes of injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Different types of force can cause injury to the bones, muscles and joints.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Direct force</strong> Damage will result at the location of the force, such as a kick or blow.</li>
<li><strong>Indirect force</strong> Damage will result away from the point where the force was applied, for example a fractured collar bone may result from landing on an outstretched arm.</li>
<li><strong>Twisting force</strong> Damage will result from torsion force on the bones and muscles, for example a twisted ankle.</li>
<li><strong>Violent movement</strong> Damage will result from sudden, violent movements, for example a knee injury from violently kicking.</li>
<li><strong>Pathological</strong> Damage will result from the bones becoming weak or brittle due to disease or old age.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-fracture">Types of fracture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A fracture is a ‘break in the continuity of the bone’. These are the basic categories for a fracture:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Closed</strong> A clean break or crack to the bone with no complications arising.</li>
<li><strong>Open</strong> A broken bone will break the skin, and may or may not still be protruding from the wound. Please bear in mind that these types of injury carry a high risk of infection.</li>
<li><strong>Complicated</strong> There are usually complications with this type of fracture, such as trapped blood vessels or nerves.</li>
<li><strong>Green stick</strong> These are more likely to occur in children who have young, more flexible bones. The bone splits, but is not completely severed. Green stick fractures can easily be mistaken for sprains and strains as only a few of the signs of fracture are present.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="dislocations">Dislocations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dislocations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A dislocation occurs when a bone becomes partially or completely dislodged at the joint, usually resulting from a wrenching movement or sudden muscular</p>
<p>Page | 52</p>
<p>contraction. The most common areas of dislocation are the jaw, thumb, knee cap, shoulder or finger.</p>
<p>Fractures can occur at or near the site of a dislocation, along with damage to ligaments, tendons and cartilage. It is sometimes difficult to distinguish between a fracture and a dislocation.</p>
<p>Never try to manipulate a dislocated joint back into place as this is best left to medical experts, and the process can be extremely painful and traumatic to the casualty. Also, you may inadvertently cause further damage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sprains-and-strains">Sprains and strains<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sprains-and-strains" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A sprain is an injury to the ligament at a joint. A strain is an injury to a muscle. These types of injury are usually caused by sudden wrenching which causes the joint to over stretch tearing the surrounding muscles and ligaments.</p>
<p>Minor fractures can be easily mistaken for a sprain or a strain. If you have any doubts, treat the injury as if it were a fracture to be on the safe side. The only way you can be sure if it is or is not a fracture is by x-ray.</p>
<p>Signs and symptoms of fracture:</p>
<p>Pain
Pain will occur at the site of the fracture. The casualty may have taken strong pain killers, have nerve damage or dementia, so be aware.</p>
<p>Loss of power
For example, the casualty will not be able to lift anything with a fractured arm.</p>
<p>Unnatural movement
If a limb is moving in an unnatural way, it is likely to be an ‘unstable’ fracture and care should be taken to prevent the fracture from moving and causing further damage.</p>
<p>Swelling and bruising
This usually occurs around the site of the fracture.</p>
<p>Deformity
If the limb is bent in the wrong place, it is broken.</p>
<p>Irregularity
There will be lumps or depressions along the surface of a bone where the broken ends overlap.</p>
<p>This document is available on
© studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Page | 54</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="crepitus">Crepitus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#crepitus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is the feeling, or sound, of bone grating on bone when the broken ends rub together.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tenderness">Tenderness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tenderness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This occurs at the site of the injury.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-a-basic-fracture">Treatment of a basic fracture:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-a-basic-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>See also:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Head injuries (see page 13)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Flail chest (see page 27)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Spinal injuries (see page 55)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reassure the casualty and tell them not to move.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use your hands to keep the injury still until it can be immobilised professionally. The casualty may be able to do this on their own, but bear in mind they may be in shock.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do not move the casualty before the injury is immobilised, unless they are in direct danger (as in the middle of a road).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do not try to bandage the injury if you have already called for an ambulance, just keep it still. If there is an open wound, you may cover it with a sterile dressing while you wait for help.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do not allow the casualty to eat, drink or smoke, as they may need surgery later.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="for-injury-to-an-upper-limb">For injury to an upper limb:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#for-injury-to-an-upper-limb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Carefully, and gently place the arm in a sling against the body. It is common to use a support sling for arm fractures. For collar bone fractures, it is common to use an elevated sling (keep the casualty’s elbow down at their side when using an elevated sling for a fractured collar bone).</li>
<li>Should the casualty be in severe pain, circulation or nerves to the arm are affected, the casualty is having problems breathing or you are at all unsure <strong>dial 999</strong> for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Arrange for the casualty to be transported to hospital.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="for-injury-to-a-lower-limb">For injury to a lower limb:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#for-injury-to-a-lower-limb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Keep the casualty still, and ensure they are kept warm. <strong>Dial 999</strong> for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>If there is any delay to the ambulance reaching you (for example you are in a remote part of the country) immobilise the injury by gently bandaging the injured leg to the uninjured one.</li>
<li>Check that their circulation has not been cut off beyond the injury and bandages. If necessary, loosen the bandages.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Treatment of sprains and strains:</p>
<p>The best way to treat sprains and strains is to follow the RICE mnemonic:</p>
<p>Rest
Ensure the casualty rests the injury. For example, do not allow a sportsman to continue (remind them it is better to miss one game than the next ten!).</p>
<p>Ice
As soon as you can, apply an ice pack (frozen vegetables such as peas or, if you have it, a refrigerant gel pack). This helps to reduce swelling and speeds recovery. Be sure not to place the ice pack directly onto the skin, as this can cause frostbite. A tea towel or any sort of thin barrier will suffice. Do this for 10 minutes every 2 hours for 24 hours for maximum effect.</p>
<p>Compression
Apply a firm (not constricting) bandage to the injured area. This also will help reduce the swelling, and provide support. The bandage can be applied over an ice pack for the first ten minutes, but be sure to remove the ice pack after this time.</p>
<p>Elevate
Elevate the injury, as this helps reduce swelling.</p>
<p>Please bear in mind that minor fractures can very easily be mistaken for a sprain or strain. The only way to be sure there is no fracture is to send or take the casualty to hospital for an x-ray.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c934eda3.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="spinal-injuries">Spinal injuries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spinal-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Approximately 2% of trauma (injury) casualties suffer spinal injury. Although this percentage appears to be fairly low, suspecting and correctly treating a spinal injury is essential. Poor treatment of a casualty with a suspected spinal injury may result in them being crippled for life, or even in death.</p>
<p>The spinal cord is an extension of the brain stem, and is located down the back of the spinal vertebrae. The spinal cord houses vital nerves which control breathing and movement. The most vulnerable part of the spinal column is the neck, and a neck injury can often be the most severe type of spinal injury. This is because as mentioned, the nerves controlling breathing are</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5 studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>housed here and may become severed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="suspected-spinal-injury">Suspected spinal injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#suspected-spinal-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Always assume the worst if the casualty has:</p>
<ul>
<li>Received a blow to the head, neck or back (this is especially important if the blow results in a loss of consciousness).</li>
<li>Fallen from a height. For example, from a horse, a ladder or off a roof.</li>
<li>Dived into shallow water.</li>
<li>Been involved in a speed accident like a car accident, or been knocked down.</li>
<li>Been in a ‘cave in’ type accident (e.g. crushing, or a scrum in rugby union).</li>
<li>Multiple injuries.</li>
<li>Any pain or tenderness to the neck or back after being involved in an accident. However, be aware that strong pain killers or other severe injuries may mask the pain that would normally alert you to a spinal injury.</li>
<li>You are in any doubt at all. Remember with this kind of injury it is better to be safe than sorry.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="possible-signs-and-symptoms-of-a-spinal-injury">Possible signs and symptoms of a spinal injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#possible-signs-and-symptoms-of-a-spinal-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Please remember</strong> – if any of the signs and symptoms below are present, nerves may already have suffered damage. Always treat a casualty that you suspect of having a spinal injury to prevent the signs and symptoms developing.</p>
<ul>
<li>Any pain or tenderness in the neck or back.</li>
<li>Any sign of a fracture to the neck or back.</li>
<li>Loss of control or sensation in limbs below the site of the injury.</li>
<li>A feeling of pins and needles, or a burning sensation in the limbs.</li>
<li>Difficulty breathing.</li>
<li>Incontinence.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-spinal-injury">Treatment of spinal injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-spinal-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the casualty is conscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tell the casualty not to move and keep reassuring them.</li>
<li>Do not allow the casualty to move or be moved, keep them in the position you found them in until help arrives. They should only be moved if they are in severe and immediate danger.</li>
<li>It is vitally important to immobilise the casualty’s head. Do this with your hands on either side and try to keep their head and neck in line with the upper body.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Keep the casualty still and warm until help arrives.</li>
</ul>
<p>If the casualty is unconscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Do not move, or allow the casualty to be moved unless they are in severe and</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>immediate danger.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Check their airway and breathing. If they are breathing fine on their own, the airway is clear so there is no need to move the head back. However, if need be you can use the ‘jaw thrust’ technique to keep the airway open without moving the head (this technique is explained on page 58). Keep a close eye on their breathing.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Using your hands, keep their head still and in line with their upper body.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If for any reason you have to leave the casualty, if they begin to vomit or if you have concerns about their airway at all, place the casualty in the recovery position. Make sure you keep the head, neck and upper body in line when you turn the casualty. To do this effectively, you will inevitably need more than one rescuer, so get local help if you can (see page 55 for instructions on how to place a suspected spinal injury casualty in the recovery position).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keep the casualty still and warm. Keep a close eye on the airway and breathing until help arrives.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>If the casualty is not breathing normally:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>If the casualty is having trouble breathing the airway will need to be opened. The head tilt may be used, but the tilt should be kept to a minimum to allow unobstructed rescue breaths to be administered.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Only if you are trained and confident, you can try to use the ‘jaw thrust’ technique. If this does not work and the casualty is still not breathing normally, you should open the airway using the head tilt method before carrying out resuscitation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Check their breathing once the airway has been opened.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If this does not help and the casualty is still having problems breathing dial 999 for an ambulance immediately, then carry out resuscitation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Try to obtain the help of others; they can support the head while you perform resuscitation.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember – if the resuscitation results in paralysis from a neck injury it is a tragedy, but failing to maintain an open airway will result in death.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="managing-the-airway-with-a-spinal-injury-casualty">Managing the airway with a spinal injury casualty<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#managing-the-airway-with-a-spinal-injury-casualty" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If an unconscious casualty is laid on their back, the airway is in danger of becoming blocked by vomit or their tongue sliding back.</p>
<p>An uninjured but unconscious casualty can simply be turned into the recovery position to help protect the airway. However, if a spinal injury is suspected, you must take great care not to move the spine.</p>
<p>Page | 57
This document is available on
5 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>If the casualty is already lying on their side (not on their backs) you may not need to move them at all. Check that the airway is not in danger of becoming blocked by vomit or their tongue. If not, keep the casualty in the position you find them.</p>
<p>Try to monitor the casualty's breathing, if it is normal you may be able to keep them still until the ambulance arrives, even if the casualty is on their back.</p>
<p>However, should the tongue slide back or the casualty vomit then immediate action is required to keep their airway clear.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="jaw-thrust">Jaw thrust:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#jaw-thrust" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the casualty is breathing, but the tongue is starting to slide back and obstruct the airway (the casualty starts making a snoring sound when they breathe) then the jaw thrust technique can be employed to keep the airway open:</p>
<ul>
<li>Kneel at the casualty's head, knees apart to keep your balance.</li>
<li>Rest your elbows on your legs (or the floor) for support and hold the casualty's head with your hands, keeping their head and neck in line with the body.</li>
<li>Place your middle and index fingers under their jaw line (under the ears).</li>
<li>Keeping their head still, lift the jaw upwards with your fingers. This action gently lifts the tongue away from the back of the throat keeping the airway clear.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2e52fddf">Do not attempt the jaw thrust technique during CPR – tilt their head back instead to open the airway.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2e52fddf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="log-roll">Log roll:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#log-roll" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If you have to leave the casualty for any reason, if they begin to vomit or you are at all concerned about their airway being clear, the casualty will have to be put on their side. Always remember to keep the head, neck and upper body in line when you turn the casualty.</p>
<p>The most effective method of turning a casualty with a suspected spinal injury is the log roll technique.</p>
<p>However, you will need at least three helpers to perform this on the casualty:</p>
<ul>
<li>Supporting the head of the casualty, keep the head, neck and upper body in line.</li>
<li>The helpers should kneel along one side of the casualty. Ask them to gently straighten the casualty's arms and legs.</li>
<li>Ensuring that you all work together, ask the helpers to roll the casualty towards them on your count of three. You should gently move the head in time with the body as the helpers roll the casualty onto their side.</li>
<li>Make sure you keep the head, neck, body and legs in line the whole time. If</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>you can, keep the casualty in this position until the ambulance arrives.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recovery-position">Recovery position:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recovery-position" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the event that you have to turn a casualty onto their side to protect their airway, but you are on your own, you will have to use the recovery position method. Keep the head, neck and body in line to the best of your ability as you roll the casualty onto their side. Have some form of padding to hand (e.g. a folded coat or jumper) to help support the casualty's head once they are on their side.</p>
<p>If you do have one or two others with you, you should keep the casualty's head supported while the helper(s) turn the casualty.</p>
<ul>
<li>Start by keeping the casualty's head supported, keeping the head, neck and upper body in line.</li>
<li>Ask your helper(s) to gently manoeuvre the casualty's arms and legs into position, ready to turn the casualty into the recovery position.</li>
<li>Ensuring that you work together, the helper(s) should roll the casualty into the recovery position. The helper(s) should pull equally on the casualty's far leg and shoulder as they turn the casualty, keeping the spine in line. You need to gently move the head to keep it in line with the upper body as the casualty is turned.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="effects-of-heat-and-cold">Effects of heat and cold<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#effects-of-heat-and-cold" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This part of the notes deals with the effects of over exposure to both heat and cold on the body.</p>
<p>Severe Hypothermia or Heat Stroke can be potentially fatal conditions and require skilful treatment from the first aider.</p>
<p>Those who are most at risk from these conditions are babies, children, the elderly or infirm and people who take part in outdoor activities like hiking, sailing or running a marathon.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="body-temperature">Body temperature<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#body-temperature" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The ideal temperature for the body to work at is <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>37</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">37^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mn>98.6</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>F</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(98.6^{\circ}F)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">98.</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">6</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">F</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>. The temperature is maintained by an area of the brain known as the 'hypothalamus'. If the body should become too hot we start to sweat, which evaporates from the skin cooling it down. Blood vessels near the surface of the skin dilate (which causes skin to flush) and the cooled blood is then circulated around the body.</p>
<p>If the body should become too cold we start to shiver, which creates heat from</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>our muscles moving. Blood vessels near the surface of the skin constrict (which causes skin to appear pale), keeping the blood closer to the warmer core of the body. The hair on the skin stands up, trapping warm air (better known as goose pimples).</p>
<p>Injuries that result from exposure to extremes of temperature can be ‘localised’ (for example sunburn and frostbite), or ‘generalised’ (for example hypothermia and heat stroke).</p>
<p>Signs and symptoms of changes to body temperature:</p>
<p>The symptoms of over-exposure to heat or cold can be seen in the table below. As the body’s temperature rises and becomes too hot or drops and becomes too cold, the hypothalamus stops working, and the condition rapidly deteriorates as the body stops fighting the condition.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Temperature</th><th>Condition</th><th>Symptoms</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>109.4-104°F / 43-40°C</td><td>Heat stroke</td><td>Unconsciousness / fitting.</td></tr><tr><td>Confused / restless.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Headache, dizzy, uncomfortable.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Strong, pounding pulse.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Flushed, dry skin, hot to the touch.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>104-100.4°F / 40-38°C</td><td>Heat exhaustion</td><td>Cramps in stomach / arms / legs.</td></tr><tr><td>Pale, sweaty skin.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Nausea / loss of appetite.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>100.4-96.8°F / 38-36°C</td><td>Normal</td><td>Normal body temperature.</td></tr><tr><td>96.8-89.6°F / 36-32°C</td><td>Mild hypothermia</td><td>Shivering.</td></tr><tr><td>Fatigue, slurred speech.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Confusion, forgetfulness.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Shivering stops, muscle rigidity.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Very slow, very weak pulse.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Noticeable drowsiness.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>87.8-75.2°F / 31-24°C</td><td>Severe hypothermia</td><td>Severe reduction in response levels.</td></tr><tr><td>Unconsciousness.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Dilated pupils.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Pulse undetectable.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Appearance of death.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Death.</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypothermia">Hypothermia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypothermia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hypothermia will occur when the core temperature of the body falls below 35°C. If the casualty is suffering from the mildest form of hypothermia, they will usually make a full recovery with professional treatment. Should the casualty’s core body temperature fall below 26°C, it will most likely be fatal. However, there have been cases of successful resuscitation of casualties with body temperatures of as low as 10°C, so it’s always worth the attempt.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The usual cause of hypothermia is over exposure to cold temperatures, but the different types of casualty and condition may have an effect on the risk:</p>
<ul>
<li>The hypothalamus of a baby or young child is underdeveloped, which can lead to hypothermia from as little as a cold room.</li>
<li>The elderly or infirm tend not to generate as much body heat so any prolonged time in the cold will lower their core temperature.</li>
<li>Wet clothes or submersion in cold water results in the body cooling much faster than when dry, as water conducts heat away from the body.</li>
<li>Inadequate clothing in windy weather will result in cold air in continuous contact with skin, resulting in the body cooling faster.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Skin appears pale and cold to the touch.</li>
<li>Initial shivering, with the body stiffening as the body cools further.</li>
<li>Bodily functions slow, including pulse, speech, breathing and thought.</li>
<li>Appears to be drunk – lethargic, confused, disorientated.</li>
<li>Lowered levels of response leading to unconsciousness and finally death.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-hypothermia">Treatment of hypothermia:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-hypothermia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the casualty is unconscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear the airway and check the casualty is breathing. If not, commence CPR.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>If the casualty is breathing, carefully place them in the recovery position. Do not move the casualty unnecessarily as the slightest jolt may cause the heart to stop.</li>
<li>Place insulating materials, such as blankets, under and around the casualty making sure to cover the head.</li>
<li>Remember to monitor the casualty’s breathing. Their pulse may be hard to locate, but it is safe to assume the casualty’s heart is beating if they are breathing.</li>
</ul>
<p>If the casualty is conscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Try to get the casualty to shelter if possible, remove any wet clothing and replace with dry, warm clothing. Cover the head, as a lot of heat is lost here.</li>
<li>If the casualty is young, fit and able then ask them to get into a warm bath <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mn>40</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><msup><mn>104</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>F</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(40^{\circ}C / 104^{\circ}F)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">4</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mord">/10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">4</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">F</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>. Do not allow an elderly casualty to do this.</li>
