<li>If the option of a bath is not viable, wrap them in warm blankets, heat the room to a warm temperature <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mn>25</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><msup><mn>77</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>F</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(25^{\circ}C / 77^{\circ}F)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mord">/7</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">7</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">F</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>.</li>
<li>If the casualty is outdoors, try to insulate them from the environment as much as possible. Use a survival bag and shelter if you have them. Also share your body heat with them.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Give the casualty something warm to drink and eat.</li>
<li>Seek medical advice if the casualty is a child, elderly or you are unsure about their condition.</li>
<li>If their condition appears severe, dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p>It is vital to NEVER:</p>
<ul>
<li>Give the casualty alcohol, as it dilates blood vessels which will cool the casualty further.</li>
<li>Place a heat source directly on or near the casualty, this will draw the blood to the skin causing the blood pressure to fall and place stress on the heart.</li>
<li>Try to warm a baby or elderly person quickly by placing them in a warm bath.</li>
</ul>
<p>A hypothermia casualty’s heart is in grave danger of ‘ventricular fibrillation’, which causes cardiac arrest. Always handle a hypothermic casualty with care as the slightest jolt can induce the condition.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frostbite">Frostbite<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frostbite" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition usually affects the extremities (such as fingers, toes, ears, etc.) when they are exposed to cold. The cells become frozen, causing ice crystals to form in the cells which in turn cause them to rupture and die. Frostbite can often be accompanied by hypothermia, which also needs to be treated. Severe frostbite can result in the loss of the affected area, normally fingers and toes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Pins and needles, numbness.</li>
<li>Skin hardens and stiffens.</li>
<li>Skin colour changes – firstly white, then blue tinge and eventually black.</li>
<li>Once recovered, the affected area will be hot, red, blistered and extremely painful.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="treatment-of-frostbite">Treatment of frostbite:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-frostbite" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Carefully remove any rings, watches, bracelets, etc.</li>
<li>Try to stop the freezing getting worse if the casualty is outdoors by placing the limb under their arm or holding it in your hands.</li>
<li>Do not rub the affected area as this will cause damage.</li>
<li>Do not try to re-warm the affected area if there is a chance of it refreezing. Move the casualty indoors before you attempt to treat them.</li>
<li>Place the affected area in warm water. Be sure to test the water yourself first, use your elbow as you would for a baby’s bath.</li>
<li>If the casualty is an adult, they may take up to two paracetamol tablets (providing they are not allergic) for intense pain.</li>
<li>Arrange transport for the casualty to hospital as soon as possible.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Page | 63</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heat-exhaustion">Heat exhaustion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heat-exhaustion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This condition is the body responding to loss of water and salt through excessive sweating. The most common cause of heat exhaustion is working or exercising in a hot temperature (such as marathon runners).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Confusion and dizziness.</li>
<li>Pale, sweaty skin.</li>
<li>Nausea, loss of appetite and vomiting.</li>
<li>Fast, weak pulse and breathing.</li>
<li>Cramping in the arms, legs and abdomen.</li>
<li>The casualty may complain of being cold, but will be hot to the touch.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-heat-exhaustion">Treatment of heat exhaustion:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-heat-exhaustion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Remove the casualty to a cool area.</li>
<li>Remove any excessive clothing and get them to lie down.</li>
<li>Give the casualty plenty of water, or preferably some form of oral re-hydration such as ‘Diaoralyte’ or isotonic drinks as this replaces their lost salt too.</li>
<li>Get medical advice, even if the casualty quickly recovers.</li>
<li>If the casualty’s response levels deteriorate, place them in the recovery position and dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. Monitor the casualty’s airway and breathing.</li>
<li>Treat the casualty for heat stroke if necessary.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heat-stroke">Heat stroke<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heat-stroke" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is a very serious condition which results from the hypothalamus failing (the temperature control centre) in the brain. The sweating mechanism fails resulting in the body being unable to cool down and the core temperature reaching a dangerously high level (over <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>40</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi>C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">40^{\circ}C</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">4</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span></span></span></span></span>) in a relatively short time (around 10 to 15 minutes).</p>
<p>Heat stroke can be caused by prolonged exposure to heat or a high fever, and usually follows heat exhaustion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Severe confusion, restlessness.</li>
<li>Flushed, dry hot skin, no sweating.</li>
<li>Strong, fast pulse.</li>
<li>Throbbing headache.</li>
<li>Dizziness.</li>
<li>Nausea and vomiting.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Response levels reduced, leading to unconsciousness.</li>
<li>Possible seizures if casualty is unconscious.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-heat-stroke">Treatment of heat stroke:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-heat-stroke" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Move the casualty to an area that is cool and shaded.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>Use whatever methods you can to cool the casualty as quickly as possible. Remove outer clothes, wrap the casualty in a cool, wet sheet. Keep the sheet wet and cold until the casualty’s temperature returns to a normal level, then remove and replace with a dry sheet.</li>
<li>Other ways to cool the casualty can include continual sponging with cold water, fanning the casualty to help the water evaporate (as would normally happen with sweat), place the casualty in a cool shower if they are conscious and able to stand, spray with water (such as with a garden hose).</li>
<li>If the casualty has a seizure, treat them as you would for a febrile convulsion (see page 69).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="taking-a-temperature">Taking a temperature<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#taking-a-temperature" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are modern, easy to use thermometers available now, such as disposable strips that you place on the forehead, and digital thermometers. Follow the manufacturer’s instructions on how to use them. If, however, you have an old fashioned mercury thermometer, the following advice may be helpful:</p>
<ul>
<li>Be careful when using the thermometer as the mercury inside is poisonous.</li>
<li>Make sure it is thoroughly cleaned before use.</li>
<li>Hold it by the end opposite to the silver, mercury bulb.</li>
<li>Shake the thermometer until the mercury falls below the 35°C mark.</li>
<li>Place it under the tongue of a conscious adult, or in the armpit of a child.</li>
<li>Keep it in place for at 3 minutes.</li>
<li>The level the mercury rises to will indicate the casualty’s temperature.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diabetes">Diabetes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diabetes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition occurs when the casualty does not produce enough of the hormone insulin naturally.</p>
<p>The body uses insulin to break down the sugar that we digest, so that it can be used by the cells or stored for later use. Basically, insulin reduces the amount of sugar in the blood.</p>
<p>Should diabetes go untreated, the casualty’s blood sugar levels will rise dangerously over 1 or 2 days (this depends on the severity of their condition).</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Diabetes is split into three different types, which are categorised by the treatment used to control it:</p>
<p><strong>Diet controlled</strong> The person still produces insulin naturally, so they can control the levels of sugar in their blood through what they eat.</p>
<p><strong>Tablet controlled</strong> The person still produces a small amount of insulin naturally, but rely on tablets to control the amount of sugar in their blood. This is usually in conjunction with a controlled diet.</p>
<p><strong>Insulin dependent</strong> The person produces little or no insulin naturally and has to administer insulin by injection twice or more a day. This helps keep the sugar levels in their blood under control.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="high-blood-sugar-hyperglycaemia">High blood sugar (hyperglycaemia)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#high-blood-sugar-hyperglycaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This occurs when the diabetes is not treated effectively with any of the methods mentioned above.</p>
<p>The sugar levels in the blood climb and acids build up. The signs and symptoms displayed by a hyperglycaemic person are a result of the body trying to excrete the acid build up.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="low-blood-sugar-hypoglycaemia">Low blood sugar (hypoglycaemia)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#low-blood-sugar-hypoglycaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition occurs mainly with diabetic people who are insulin dependent, as the level of insulin in the body is 'fixed' due to the dose administered by injection.</p>
<p>Because the amount of insulin is fixed, they must balance it with the amount of food they eat.</p>
<p>Blood sugar levels fall if:</p>
<ul>
<li>The person has not eaten enough food.</li>
<li>The person over exerts (burns off the sugar in their blood).</li>
<li>The person has injected a too high dose of insulin.</li>
</ul>
<p>Why low blood sugar is dangerous:</p>
<p>Unlike the other cells in the body, glucose (sugar) is the only energy source the brain can use. If the sugar in the blood drops, this literally starves the brain.</p>
<p>Page | 65
This document is available on
□
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Please refer to the table below for signs and symptoms of both low and high blood sugar. The result is the 'hungry' brain cells become disordered, causing the release of adrenaline into the body (see 'the body's response to hypoxia', see page 16).</p>
<p>Signs and symptoms:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>High blood sugar (hyperglycaemia)</th><th>Low blood sugar (hypoglycaemia)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Onset</td><td>Slow – 12 to 48 hours</td><td>Fast – 2 mins to 1 hour</td></tr><tr><td>Level of response</td><td>Deterioration is slow during onset:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Drowsy, lethargic.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Unconsciousness if left untreated.</td><td>Deterioration is fast during onset:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Weak, dizzy.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Confused, memory loss.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Lack of coordination.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Slurred speech.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Bizarre, uncharacteristic, un-cooperative, possible violence.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Unconscious within 1 hour.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Skin</td><td>Dry and warm.</td><td>Pale, cold and sweaty.</td></tr><tr><td>Breathing</td><td>Deep, sighing breaths.</td><td>Normal to shallow and rapid.</td></tr><tr><td>Pulse</td><td>Rapid.</td><td>Rapid.</td></tr><tr><td>Other symptoms</td><td>Excessive urination.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Excessive thirst.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Hunger.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Fruity odour on the breath.</td><td>Please note – the signs can be easily confused with intoxication.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Treatment of high blood sugar (hyperglycaemia):</p>
<ul>
<li>Arrange for the casualty to see a doctor as a matter of urgency.</li>
<li>Should the casualty become unconscious, maintain their airway and breathing, place in the recovery position. Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment of low blood sugar (hypoglycaemia):</p>
<p>If the casualty is conscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Sit the casualty in a comfortable position.</li>
<li>Give the casualty a sugary drink (isotonic sports drinks are the best option as they are high in sugar), sugar lumps, glucose tablets, chocolate or other sweet foods.</li>
<li>If the casualty responds quickly to this treatment, wait five minutes then give them some carbohydrates (a sandwich, pasta, etc.).</li>
<li>Stay with the casualty, do not leave them alone. Let them rest until they are fully alert and their response levels are back to normal.</li>
<li>Suggest to the casualty they see their doctor, even if they appear to have fully recovered.</li>
<li>If the casualty does not respond to the treatment within 10 minutes, or they become unmanageable, dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Consider the possibility that there may be another cause for the casualty’s symptoms.</li>
</ul>
<p>If the casualty is unconscious:</p>
<ul>
<li>Open the airway and check for breath. Resuscitate if necessary.</li>
<li>Place the casualty in the recovery position if they are breathing normally.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epilepsy">Epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>People with this condition have a tendency to have seizures (fits) that come from a disturbance in the brain. However, bear in mind that one in twenty people will experience a seizure at some point in their lives, so the casualty may not be epileptic.</p>
<p>The causes of a seizure are many, such as hypoxia, stroke, a head injury or even a high body temperature.</p>
<p>Seizures are common in babies and children whose temperature is too high due to illness and fever. This is covered under the heading ‘febrile convulsions’, which follows.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="minor-seizures">Minor seizures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#minor-seizures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition is also known as ‘absence seizures’ or ‘petit mal’ seizures. The person may suddenly appear to be daydreaming (even in mid-sentence). This may only last a couple of seconds before recovery, and the person may not even realise what just happened. On some occasions a minor seizure may be accompanied by unusual movements such as twitching in the face, jerking of a limb or lip smacking. The person can at times make a noise such as a sudden cry.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-minor-seizures">Treatment of minor seizures:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-minor-seizures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Remove any danger near the casualty, such as cutlery or hot drinks they may be holding.</li>
<li>Help the casualty to sit in a quiet place, and reassure them.</li>
<li>Stay with them until they are fully alert.</li>
<li>If the casualty is unaware of any condition, suggest they see their doctor.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="major-seizures">Major seizures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#major-seizures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the more serious type of seizure, resulting from a major disturbance in the brain which causes aggressive fitting throughout the body.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>These types of fit can be very frightening to see, but try to remain calm as prompt action is essential for the casualty.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is usually a pattern to a major seizure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aura">Aura<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aura" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the casualty has had a history of seizures, they may recognise when a fit is imminent. The warning signs (or aura) could include a taste in the mouth, a smell or a peculiar feeling. This warning may give them sufficient time to seek help or simply find somewhere quiet to lie down before they fall.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tonic-phase">'Tonic' phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tonic-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This phase is where every muscle in the body goes rigid. The casualty may emit a cry and fall to the floor. Their back may arch and their lips may turn blue (cyanosis). This typically lasts less than 20 seconds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clonic-phase">'Clonic' phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clonic-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The casualty's limbs make sudden, violent jerking movements, their eyes may roll, they may clench their teeth, they may drool from the mouth (sometimes it can be bloody due to biting their tongue) and there could be a 'snoring' sound when they breathe. Also, the casualty may lose control of their bladder and/or bowel.</p>
<p>The clonic phase can last from 30 seconds to hours, but most stop within a couple of minutes. If the seizure lasts more than 15 minutes, or they have a number of consecutive seizures, it is a dire medical emergency and should be treated as such.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recovery-phase">Recovery phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recovery-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is when the body relaxes even though the casualty may still be unresponsive. Their response levels will improve within a few minutes, but they may not be fully alert for about 20 minutes or so. They may want to sleep to recuperate, and be unaware of their actions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-major-seizures-fits">Treatment of major seizures (fits):<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-major-seizures-fits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During the seizure:</p>
<ul>
<li>If you can, help the casualty to the floor to avoid injury.</li>
<li>Try to cushion the casualty's head, to avoid head trauma. This can be accomplished with a folded coat, jumper or even your hands.</li>
<li>Loosen any tight clothing around their neck to help them breathe.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Try to move any objects from the area around the casualty, to lessen the risk of them hurting themselves, and ask bystanders to move away if necessary.</li>
<li>If you have any concerns about their airway, roll them onto their side.</li>
<li>Take an exact note of the time the seizure started and how long it lasts.</li>
<li>If the casualty is a stranger to you, check for any identification.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dial-999-for-an-ambulance-if">Dial 999 for an ambulance if:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dial-999-for-an-ambulance-if" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The seizure lasts for more than 3 minutes.</li>
<li>Once the seizure has run its course, if their response levels don't improve within 10 minutes.</li>
<li>The casualty has a second seizure.</li>
<li>If the casualty has no history of epilepsy, or this is their first major seizure.</li>
<li>You do not know the casualty.</li>
<li>You are at all unsure.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="as-soon-as-the-seizure-stops">As soon as the seizure stops:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#as-soon-as-the-seizure-stops" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Check the casualty's airway and breathing. Resuscitate if necessary.</li>
<li>Place the casualty in the recovery position.</li>
<li>Try to keep the casualty warm (unless a high temperature caused the seizure initially) and reassure them.</li>
<li>Monitor their airway and breathing.</li>
<li>Move any bystanders away before the casualty comes to, and try to preserve their dignity.</li>
<li>Keep a close eye on their response levels and dial 999 for an ambulance if their condition doesn't improve within 10 minutes, or if you are unsure at all.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="febrile-convulsions">Febrile convulsions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#febrile-convulsions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The part of the brain that regulates temperature (the hypothalamus) is not yet fully developed in babies and young children, which can lead to the body's core temperature reaching dangerously high levels, which may lead to a child having a fit.</p>
<p>A child having a febrile convulsion can be a very frightening and distressing sight for a parent or guardian. When the child is in the 'tonic' phase, s/he may stop breathing as the diaphragm goes rigid resulting in the lips and face turning blue (cyanosis). It is vital, therefore, to remain as calm and reassuring as possible in this situation.</p>
<p>The child may have been unwell recently and be hot to the touch.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-febrile-convulsions">Treatment of febrile convulsions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-febrile-convulsions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Remove any clothing and bedclothes from the child. Try to provide fresh, cool</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
5 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>air to help reduce the child’s temperature. Be careful not to overly cool the child however.</p>
<ul>
<li>To protect the airway, place the child in the recovery position (on their side) if it is possible.</li>
<li>Remove any dangers from around the child and use some padding to ensure the child isn’t hurt during the fit. It is very important to protect the child’s head.</li>
<li>Dial 999 for an ambulance immediately.</li>
<li>If the child continues to fit, use a sponge with tepid water to help the cooling process. However, do not cool them down too much.</li>
<li>Keep a close eye on the child’s airway and breathing until the ambulance arrives.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Please remember:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Never place anything in a fitting casualty’s mouth, especially your fingers.</li>
<li>Never try to restrain them or hold them down – allow the fit to run its course.</li>
<li>Never move a fitting casualty unless they are in imminent danger.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="health-safety-first-aid-regulations-1981">Health &amp; Safety (first aid) Regulations 1981<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-safety-first-aid-regulations-1981" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><strong>Employer’s responsibility:</strong></p>
<p>It is the responsibility of the employer to make sure sufficient first aid provision is made in the workplace, under Health &amp; Safety law. This includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Assessing the first aid needs of the workplace – how many first aiders are needed, what type of training is required, are they following the guidance from the HSE (Health &amp; Safety Executive).</li>
<li>Provide training and re-qualification courses for the appointed first aiders.</li>
<li>Ensure there are enough first aid kits and equipment for the workplace, and that they are well stocked.</li>
<li>Ensure that all staff are aware who the first aiders are and where to find them should they need treatment.</li>
</ul>
<p>Following is advice for employers on their responsibilities, but first aid training organisations are usually happy to give further advice if needed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="first-aid-kits">First aid kits<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#first-aid-kits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The first aid kit should be easily accessible; preferably near somewhere the first aider can wash their hands. The kits should be easily identified by a large white cross on a green background. The container should protect the contents from dust and damp.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>First aid kits should be available at all workplaces. However, larger sites will need more than one first aid kit to cover the greater amount of people. The kits should contain the following, as a guide only:</p>
<ul>
<li>A leaflet with general guidance on first aid.</li>
<li>20 individually wrapped sticking plasters of assorted sizes and absorption, suited to the type of work (e.g., blue plasters are to be used by food handlers, as they are highly visible). Hypoallergenic plasters can be provided too.</li>
<li>2 sterile eye pads.</li>
<li>4 individually wrapped triangular bandages, preferably sterile.</li>
<li>6 safety pins.</li>
<li>6 individually wrapped medium, sterile wound dressings (approx. 12cm x 12cm).</li>
<li>2 individually wrapped large, sterile wound dressings (approx. 18cm x 18cm).</li>
<li>1 pair of disposable gloves. Please remember some people are allergic to latex, so try to find an alternative (such as Nitrile).</li>
</ul>
<p>The above contents are not mandatory and equivalent items can be used. You can also add items such as scissors, adhesive tape, disposable aprons, individually wrapped moist wipes, face shields, etc. Keep them in the first aid kit, if there is room, or nearby if not.</p>
<p>You may also wish to consider keeping other equipment close to the first aid kit such as blankets (to help protect casualties from the elements, keep them warm if they are in shock, etc.), breathing apparatus (should the first aider need to enter a dangerous atmosphere), etc.</p>
<p>Eye wash:</p>
<p>If there is no access to tap water for eye irrigation, there should be at least 1 litre of sterile water, or ‘saline’ solution available in a sealed, disposable container(s).</p>
<p>Travelling first aid kits</p>
<p>If the employees travel, it is advisable to provide them with a first aid kit, which will typically include:</p>
<ul>
<li>A leaflet with general guidance on first aid.</li>
<li>6 individually wrapped sterile plasters.</li>
<li>2 individually wrapped triangular bandages, preferably sterile.</li>
<li>2 safety pins.</li>
<li>1 individually wrapped large, sterile wound dressing (approx. 18cm x 18cm).</li>
<li>Individually wrapped moist cleansing wipes.</li>
<li>1 pair of disposable gloves. Please remember some people are allergic to latex, so try to find an alternative (such as Nitrile).</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
© studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Page | 72</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="first-aid-needs-assessment">First aid needs assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#first-aid-needs-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Employers must carry out a first aid needs assessment, which should answer the following questions:</p>
<ul>
<li>What is the nature of the work? What are the hazards and risks of the workplace?</li>
<li>What is the size of the organisation?</li>
<li>What is the nature of the workforce?</li>
<li>What is the organisation’s history of illness and accidents?</li>
<li>What are the needs of travelling, remote or lone workers, if applicable?</li>
<li>What are the work patterns (such as shift work)?</li>
<li>What is the distribution of the workforce?</li>
<li>How far away is the closest emergency medical service (medical centre, etc.)?</li>
<li>How many employees are there working on shared or multi-occupied sites?</li>
<li>Are there enough first aiders to cover each other over annual leave and other absences?</li>
<li>Is there first aid provision for non-employees?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="workplace-hazards-and-risks">Workplace hazards and risks<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#workplace-hazards-and-risks" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>One of the more difficult areas of a first aid needs assessment is taking into consideration the nature of the work and workplace hazards and risks.</p>
<p>Employers should take into consideration the risks and identify the possible injuries which could occur in order to ensure the first aid provision is sufficient. The table below, compiled using information from the HSE, should help in identifying some common workplace risks, and the possible resulting injuries:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Risk</th><th>Possible injuries</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Manual Handling</td><td>Fracture, laceration, strain and sprain</td></tr><tr><td>Slip / Trip Hazard</td><td>Fracture, laceration, strain and sprain</td></tr><tr><td>Machinery</td><td>Crush injury, amputation, fracture, eye injury, laceration</td></tr><tr><td>Work at Height</td><td>Head injury, unconsciousness, spinal injury, fracture, strain and sprain</td></tr><tr><td>Workplace Transport</td><td>Crush injury, fracture, spinal injury, strain and sprain</td></tr><tr><td>Electricity</td><td>Electric shock, burns</td></tr><tr><td>Chemicals</td><td>Poison, unconsciousness, burns, eye injury</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>All these aspects should be taken into consideration by the employer to determine how many people should be trained in first aid, and to what level to ensure their workplace is safely covered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="first-aiders">First aiders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#first-aiders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An employer should consider a number of things when choosing an employee to be a first aider. Ideally, a first aider will have the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reliability, good disposition and communication skills</li>
<li>An aptitude and ability to absorb new skills and knowledge</li>
<li>An ability to cope with stressful and physically demanding emergency procedure</li>
<li>Normal workplace duties that can be left if an emergency situation arises</li>
</ul>
<p>As of October 2009, there have been introduced a new training regime for first aiders. The HSE (Health &amp; Safety Executive) have introduced two levels of first aider:</p>
<ul>
<li>First Aider at Work</li>
<li>Emergency First Aider at Work</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contents-of-hse-first-aid-courses">Contents of HSE First Aid Courses:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contents-of-hse-first-aid-courses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<table><thead><tr><th>Heart attack</th><th>X</th><th>Y</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Stroke</td><td>X</td><td>Y</td></tr><tr><td>Epilepsy</td><td>X</td><td>Y</td></tr><tr><td>Asthma</td><td>X</td><td>Y</td></tr><tr><td>Diabetes</td><td>X</td><td>Y</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-426df1f9.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Please note: If the level of first aid assessment identifies that the workplace needs first aid at work training (3 day course), it is not acceptable to provide emergency first aid at work training (1 day course).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="annual-refresher-training">Annual refresher training<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#annual-refresher-training" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The HSE recommend that all employees trained in first aid attend an annual refresher course due to the evidence on the severity of ‘first aid skill fade’.</p>
<p>The above flow chart shows the HSE’s recommended sequence of training.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reporting-incidents-at-work">Reporting incidents at work<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reporting-incidents-at-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All accidents in the workplace must be recorded in an accident book, no matter how small the resulting injury. The incident may need to be reported to the HSE under RIDDOR regulations.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="riddor-1995-regulations">RIDDOR 1995 regulations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#riddor-1995-regulations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Reporting of Injuries, Diseases and Dangerous Occurrences Regulations 1995.</p>
<p>These regulations specify that it is the responsibility of the employer or person in control of the premises to report the following incidents directly to the HSE:</p>
<ul>
<li>Death (report immediately)</li>
<li>Major injury (report immediately)</li>
<li>Dangerous occurrences (report immediately)</li>
<li>Incident resulting in a person being off work (or unable to carry out full duties) for more than 3 days (report within 10 days)</li>
<li>Diseases (report asap)</li>
</ul>
<p>For further information, please visit the RIDDOR website at <a href="http://www.riddor.gov.uk" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.riddor.gov.uk</a>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="accident-book">Accident book<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#accident-book" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An accident at work must be recorded in an accident book, no matter how severe the injury. The accident book may be filled in by anyone on behalf of the casualty, or if they are capable the casualty themselves.</p>
<p>The recorded information can help the employer to identify accident trends and possible areas for improvement in control of health and safety risks. It can also be used for future first aid needs, assessments and could also be helpful for insurance investigation purposes.</p>
<p>The first aider is often the one to fill in an accident book, so the following notes are given as advice:</p>
<ul>
<li>An accident book is a legal document</li>
<li>Any information recorded in the accident book is usually considered to be ‘stronger evidence’ in court than something recalled from memory</li>
<li>Complete the report in the book at the same time, using the same pen (not pencil)</li>
<li>In compliance with the Data Protection Act, all personal information entered in the accident book must be kept confidential. With this in mind, the accident book should be one where</li>
</ul>
<p>Page | 75</p>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>the pages can be removed to be stored securely</p>
<ul>
<li>There should be one member of staff nominated to be responsible for the safekeeping of completed accident reports (e.g. in a secure cabinet). Always hand the completed accident report to that person</li>
<li>The casualty may wish to take a photocopy of the accident report. If this is the case, they can take a copy before the report is handed in. They should keep a record of the accident report number</li>
</ul>
<p>You should include in your report:</p>
<ul>
<li>The name, address and occupation of the casualty</li>
<li>The name, address, occupation and signature of the person completing the report</li>
<li>The date, time and location the accident occurred</li>
<li>A description of how the accident happened, include the cause if known</li>
<li>Details of the injury sustained</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="first-aid-casualty-report-form">First aid casualty report form<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#first-aid-casualty-report-form" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is useful for the first aider to complete a patient report form for all casualties. Please note that this is not in place of the accident report book, which still has to be completed for any accidents in the workplace.</p>
<p>The casualty report form is designed to help the first aider keep an accurate record of the exact treatment provided. It is especially useful if the casualty refuses treatment against the advice of the first aid provider.</p>
<ul>
<li>If the casualty refuses treatment, make sure that they are capable of making that decision (e.g. they are a fully conscious adult). If they are not capable of making the decision, seek medical advice</li>
<li>Follow the advice given for completing the accident report book when completing the form</li>
<li>A copy of the completed form can be given to ambulance or hospital staff as it will contain valuable details on the incident, treatment and casualty. Ask the nurse to take a copy so you can retain the original for your records</li>
<li>To comply with the Data Protection Act, all personal details on the report must remain confidential, so the report should be securely stored (e.g. in a secure cabinet)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="avpu-score">AVPU score:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#avpu-score" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The AVPU scale is the simplest way to record the conscious level of a casualty. A detailed explanation can be found on page 9.</p>
<p>The scale should also be on the casualty report form, so you don't have to memorise it. There is a score provided for each level of consciousness. Note the score in the observations chart each time you measure it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a698fa03">Appendix: Resuscitation – child over 1 year:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a698fa03" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>NOTE: This section deals with the differences between adult and child resuscitation.</p>
<p>REMEMBER: If you are at all unsure, it is better to perform the adult sequence on a child who is unresponsive and not breathing than not to do anything at all.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="child-resuscitation">Child resuscitation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#child-resuscitation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="danger">Danger<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#danger" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Make sure you are safe to help, do not put yourself in danger</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="response">Response<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Gently tap the child’s shoulders and shout ‘are you alright?’</li>
<li>If the child does not respond shout for help, but don’t leave the child yet</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="airway">Airway<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#airway" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Carefully ensure the airway is open by gently tipping the child’s head back with the ‘chin lift’:</p>
<ul>
<li>Place your hand on the child’s forehead and gently tilt their head back</li>
<li>Using your fingertips under the point of the chin, lift the chin to open the airway</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="breathing">Breathing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#breathing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ensuring the airway is kept open; look, listen and feel to ascertain whether the child is breathing normally. Do not take more than ten seconds to do this.</p>
<ul>
<li>If the child is able to breath normally, carry out a secondary survey and place the child in the recovery position (see page 12)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="if-the-child-is-not-breathing-normally">If the child is not breathing normally:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#if-the-child-is-not-breathing-normally" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>If you are not alone, ask the nearest person to dial 999 for an ambulance immediately. If you are alone and need to leave the child to ensure help is on the way, perform resuscitation for about 1 minute first:</li>
<li>Maintain the child’s airway by tilting their head and lifting the chin</li>
<li>Pinch the fleshy part of the nose and seal your mouth around the child’s</li>
<li>Give 5 initial rescue breaths. Blow in just enough air to make the child’s chest rise visibly</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="combine-rescue-breaths-with-chest-compressions">Combine rescue breaths with chest compressions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#combine-rescue-breaths-with-chest-compressions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Using 1 or 2 hands as required to depress the child’s chest a third of its depth</li>
<li>Give 30 chest compressions at a rate of 100 compressions per minute</li>
<li>Open the airway again by tilting the child’s head and lifting the chin, and give 2 more rescue breaths</li>
<li>Continue to administer 30 chest compressions followed by 2 rescue breaths</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="e1c834db">If your rescue breaths do not make the child’s chest rise effectively:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#e1c834db" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Give a further 30 chest compressions before attempting the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Check inside the mouth and remove any clearly visible obstruction (do not reach blindly into the child’s throat)</li>
<li>Recheck that the head is adequately tilted back and the chin is lifted</li>
<li>Do not administer more than 2 rescue breaths before resuming chest compressions</li>
</ul>
<p>Note: If there is another rescuer with you, change over every two minutes to prevent either of you getting fatigued. Ensure as short a delay as possible as you change over.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6aae375a">Appendix: Resuscitation – baby under 1 year:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6aae375a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>NOTE: This section deals with the differences between adult and baby resuscitation.</p>
<p><strong>REMEMBER</strong>: If you are at all unsure, it is better to perform the adult sequence on a child who is unresponsive and not breathing than not to do anything at all.</p>
